0.1 Background
Union Miniere (UM) is a multinational ccmpany with headquarters in Belgium, which specialises
in the production of non-ferrous metals. The company is interested in acquiring a majority
shareholding in the MDK JS Company, which is one of the Bulgarian State owned companies
given priority for privatisation. MDK operates a copper smelter/refinery complex located close to
the town of Pirdop in Bulgaria.
Prior to final negotiations with the Privatisation Agency and submission of a non-binding offer,
Union Mini&re was permitted access to MDK to carry out a preliminary due diligence. Hatch
Associates Limited (Hatch), an international consultancy specialising in metallurgical and
environmental engineering,  was appointed by Union Miniere to perform  a preliminary
environmental assessment of the MDK operations and the site. This was carried out during a
site inspection in the week commencing 23 March 1997; the findings of the assessment were
documented in a Hatch report entitled Preliminary Environmental Review of the MDK Copper
Smelter.
Following submission of an offer for MDK, Union Miniere was selected as the Preferred Investor
by the Bulgarian Privatisation Agency on 9 June 1997. Under the terms of the Privatisation
Agency, Union Miniere was allowed a period of 2 to 3 months to complete the due diligence.
Hatch was subsequently retained by Union Miniere to complete a full environmental assessment
of MDK during the period of exclusivity.
0.2 Objectives, Terms of Reference
The preliminary investigation highlighted several areas of non-compliance and potential liability
which require further investigation. These are summarised as follows:
Air emissions
- emissions of sulphur dioxide and sulphur trioxide from the 120 metre stack exceed
maximum acceptable values
- emissions of dust from the 325 metre stack exceed maximum acceptable concentrations
- measurements in the town of Pirdop and Zlatitsa indicate that fugitive emissions from the
smelter operation and open storage areas on the MDK site contribute to poor air quality in
these regions.
PR90579 Rev. 0. G5d0                                                 PR.DOT; Rev. 3, 9515
.lunonmn190579WczUxpr90579 coc



Union Miniere - EIA of MDK Smelter
HATCH                                                                            Page 5
Waste-water
site drainage waters which influx an important river system frequently exceed maximum
acceptable concentration limits for heavy metals and may also be contaminated with
suspended solids and oil
acidic process waste streams are treated by a simple lime neutralisation which is not
considered Best Available Techniques (BAT). Treated water is always in excess of
maximum acceptable concentration limits for a number of substances. Furthermore a
hazardous calcium arsenite/sulphate sludge is produced which requires safe disposal.
Solid waste disposal
a large pond used for settling the calcium arsenite sludge is full and there are doubts
concerning its stability in the short and long term
altemative safe sites are required for disposal of the calcium arsenite sludge
the slag tailings pond, located above the village of Pirdop, is almost full and its physical
stability under certain conditions is not known
solid waste of varying compositions, contaminated used equipment and disused structures
are on the site and will ultimately require removal to a secure deposit
there is a potential for soil and groundwater contamination as a result of seepage from the
slag dumps and other contaminrated storage areas.
The general objectives of the full environmental assessment are to:
assess the extent of environmental pollution resulting from previous operations at MDK
evaluate the current situation with respect to air emissions, liquid effluents and storage of
solid waste materials
As far as the historical soil contamination and waste disposal is concemed : provide an
indication of the technologies, equipment or remediation work required to bring MDK's
operations into compliance with Bulgarian and draft European BAT standards (Osparcom).
The costs and schedule for implementation are to be estimated
An emission reduction planning is presented in this report for air and liquid effluents.
A waste management system is proposed for the production and safe disposal of waste.

0.3 Legal Requirements
The assessment is to be performed in accordance with the requirements of the Environmental
Protection Act and specifically with Regulation No. 1 of 7 August 1995 on Environmental Impact
Assessment issued by The Republic of Bulgaria Ministry of Environment, Ministry of Territorial
Development and Construction, and the Ministry of Agriculture and Food Industry.
The general scope of work and methodology for the EIA was submitted to and approved by the
Ministry of Environment at a Plenary Meeting at the Ministry of Environment on 26 June 1997.
In order to complete the work in the scheduled time period, and because of the requirement for
specialised equipment, Hatch has engaged a Bulgarian Company, Engineering Service Sofia
Ltd (ESS). This company provides technical experts in various fields to carry out measurements
and analysis of emissions from the site and to perform geotechnical survey work. ESS and its
experts are approved by the Ministry of Environment.
Under the terms of the privatisation programme, the Bulgarian State will retain responsibility for
the historical pollution both on the site and in the surrounding region. The World Bank has a
provisional agreement with the Bulgarian Government that financial assistance, in the form of a
loan to the Bulgarian Government, may be available to aid the remedial work.
0.4 Sources of Information
Information used to compile this report has been obtained from a variety of sources which are
summarised as follows:
- visual observations, on-site surveys, measurements and analyses by Hatch and ESS during
June and July 1997

1. GENERAL INFORMATION
1.1 Company Details
The address of the company under assessment is:
MDK Copper Smelter & Refinery J.S. Company
2070 Pirdop
Bulgaria
Telephone number:   7181 57 17
The Executive Director is Mr Alexander Alexandrov.
MDK JS Company is 100% owned by the Bulgarian State
1.2 History of the Facility
Construction of copper smelting facilities at the site near Pirdop commenced in 1956 and the
plant was commissioned in 1958. The initial plant treated copper ore and concentrate from local
mines using a process consisting of:
- fluidised bed roasting
=- electric furnace smelting
reverberatory fumace converting.
The facilities included a sulphuric acid plant and an electrorefinery together with auxiliary plants
for services such as steam and oxygen.
During the mid 1960s the capacity of the plant was increased with the addition of a roasting
furnace with a hearth area of 25m2 and a 6 electrode, electric arc fumace and two new acid
plants. The refinery was also expanded to give a capacity of 64 000 tonnes per year cathode
copper.
Between 1967 and 1987 further improvements were carried out with the installation of an
effluent treatment plant, a new sulphuric acid plant and the slag flotation process.
The smelting process was completely revised in the mid 1 980s with the installation of
Outokumpu flash smelting technology and Pierce Smith converters. This facility, which is
installed in the north-west part of the site was commissioned in 1987. A list of the major
production and auxiliary units is given in Table 1.2-1.

Former planned improvements by MDK, include the installation of a new double contact, double
absorption sulphuric acid plant and a new copper electrorefinery. Foundations for these were
completed, but lack of money prevented installation of the equipment.
The old facilities which occupy the south east area of the site were not removed after the
installation of the flash smelting process. The buildings and equipment are in various stages of
decay and are contaminated with process and waste materials.
The Outokumpu furnace has never operated at its full design capacity due to:
- shortage of suitable feed material
' insufficient sulphuric acid plant capacity.

1.3 Site Description
The smelter occupies a site with a total area of 426 hectares; the smelter boundary iimits
covering an area of approximately 149 hectares. Approximately 47 hectares are occupied by
constructions or buildings. Boundaries of two urban centres, Pirdop and Zlatitsa, lie close to the
eastern and western perimeters of the MDK site. The location of the site with respect to the
local urban centres, transport infrastructure and geographical features are shown in Figure
1.3-1.
A layout plan of the site showing the main operating units and storage areas is given in Dwg.
No. 90579-001 given in Appendix B.
The present operating units occupy an area of approximately 80 hectares in the western half of
the site. Disused plant and equipment covers an area of approximately 40 hectares in the
centre of the site. In addition, a significant area is utilised for the storage of a variety of waste
materials which include:
a slag pile - smelting slag from MDK's previous operations
- slag tailings pond -fayalite tailings from the slag flotation process
> slime pond - containing calcium sulphate/arsenite sludge produced by the liquid effluent
treatment process.
The MDK smelter and surrounding area are illustrated on photographs in Appendix A.

1.4 Description of the Region Affected by the Facility
The MDK Smelter complex occupies a site in the central part of Bulgaria within the valley of two
rivers, the Zlatishka and the Pirdopska. The valley, which runs in an approximate east-west
direction, is about 29 km long and 4 km wide. It is surrounded by mountains of the Balkan
Range to the North and Hills of the Sredna Gora Range to the south. The altitude of the site is
between about 700 and 750 metres above sea level and the surrounding hills rise to a height of
up to 1500 metres.
The area is located about 85km east of the capital city, Sofia and 270 km west of the Black Sea.
A map of the region showing the location of the area in relation to Sofia is given in Figure 1.4-1.
The MDK site occupies an area on the north slope of the valley between the confluence of the
rivers Zlatishka and Pirdopska. The Pirdopska then joins the River Topolnitza which flows in a
Southerly direction towards the Plovdiv region. This is an important river system with respect to
the population and agriculture in the farmlands of central Bulgaria.

1.5 Location
1.5.1 Population
The overall population of the former Srednogori community was 25 040 in 1992 with the majority
living in the small towns of Pirdop and Zlatitsa. Pirdop is approximately 1000 metres from the
site perimeter in a south-easterly direction; Zlatitsa is approximately 500 metres to the west of
MDK's perimeter The location of these urban centres in relation to MDK is shown in Figure
1.3.1
The distribution of population in the region is given in Table 1.5.1
Table 1.5-1: Distribution of population in the Region, 1992."

1.5.2 Industry
The Zlatitsa-Pirdop valley lies in the centre of a major ore mining industry which includes:
- Panaguurski copper mines
- Assarel-Medet copper mines
BIMAK - a joint venture between Irish mining company Navan Resources and Chelopech
copper mine
> Elatzite copper mine.
These together with the MDK smelter employ more than 5000 people from the region (more
than 54 percent of the working population). MDK employs about 2500 in total. Other industries
in the region include:
- brick and tile manufacture
- detergent manufacture
- food and meat processing
clothing

The valley is an important agricultural area for cereal crops, fruit and vegetables and for rearing
beef cattle, pigs and sheep.
The Balkan and Sredna Gora mountains provide tourism and recreational facilities and there are
a number of chalets and rest houses in the region.
1.5.3 Infrastructure
The region is served by the sub-Balkan railway track which passes through the southern
perimeter of MDK and has stations in Pirdop and Zlatitsa. Spurs into the MDK site are used for
receiving raw materials and dispatching finished copper and sulphuric acid by rail.
Highways connect the region with the capital city Sofia in the west, Plovdiv and Smolyon in the
south and Oriahove on the Danube in the north.
There is a plentiful supply of industrial water from the Duchantzi dam reservoir which regulates
the flow of the Topolnitza to the north east of the site. The dam is owned and maintained by
MDK and has a capacity of 11 million cubic metres.

2. CHARACTERISTICS OF THE FACILITY
2.1 Process Description
A simplified flowsheet of the copper smelting and refining process at MDK is given in Figure 2.1.
The major unit operations consist of:
- raw material receipt, handling and storage
- charge preparation
- flash smelting
- conversion of matte to blister copper
copper-refining and anode casting
electrolytic refining
precious metals recovery
sulphuric acid production
slag treatment
- waste water cleaning
services
Brief descriptions of the process and equipment of each unit operation are given in the following
sections.
2.1. 1 Raw Material Receipt, Handling and Storage
Copper sulphide concentrates are received by truck from local mines at Assarel Medet and
Elatzite. The concentrates contains typically 20 percent copper and 30 percent sulphur.
Imported concentrates are purchased via intemational trading companies or treated under
tolling contracts. This material is received in Bulgaria at unloading facilities at a Black Sea port
and transferred to MDK by rail transport.
Concentrates, which contain about 10 to 15 percent moisture, are stored either in dedicated
buildings or in open storage areas.
2.1.2 Feed Preparation
Prior to smelting, different concentrates are blended to form a charge which provides as
consistent as possible feed to the flash smelting furnace. The feedstock, which comprises
concentrates, flux materials and recycled copper materials, must be dried to a moisture content
of approximately 0.2 percent before it is suitable for charging to the smelting furnace.

Figure 2.1   SIMPLIFIED FLOWSHEET OF THE SMELTING /REFINING PROCESS 



ztl~                                                      Union Mini re - EIA of MDK Smelter
HATCH                                                                         Page 19
Drying is carried out in two rotary kilns. Each kiln is 34 metres long by 3.4 metres diameter and
heated by heavy oil burners with capacity of 72 kg oil per hour. The capacity of each kiln is
approximately 70 tonnes per hour of dry concentrate assuming on initial moisture content of 11
percent.
Off-gases from the kilns are cleaned in Lurgi electrostatic precipitators, one for each kiln, each
having a cleaning capacity of 72 000 Nm3 per hour.
2.1.3 Flash Smelting
Dry concentrate together with silica flux, recycled precipitator dusts and converter slag are fed
by conveyors to the feed hoppers of the flash furnace. From the hoppers, the charge material is
fed to the bumers in the reaction shaft of the Outokumpu flash furnace. Design parameters of
the fumace are as follows:
Capacity                    120 tonnes per hour concentrate
Concentrate burners         4
Reaction shaft              8.23 metre high x 7 metre diameter
Settler                     26.95 x 8.7 metre
Uptake                       10.4 metre high x 5.2 metre diameter
In the reaction shaft, concentrates react with oxygen enriched air and are partially oxidised to
copper matte with a grade of about 50 percent copper, 23 percent sulphur. Part of the iron
sulphide is oxidised and combines with gangue components and fluxes to form a slag with a
copper content of 1 to 2 percent. Sulphur dioxide and sulphur trioxide are gaseous products of
the oxidation reaction. The exothermic reaction provides sufficient energy to melt the matte and
slag which fall into the furnace settler where the two phases separate and are tapped from the
fumace.
Slag is tapped into ladles and transported by rail to cooling pits; matte is tapped into transfer
ladles and carried by overhead travelling crane to the converter furnaces.
Off-gases from the fumace are cooled and partially cleaned in a waste-heat boiler which cools
the gases from about 13000C to 4000C. Some particulate matter is also removed from the gas
stream during passage through the waste heat boiler . After cooling the gas stream passes
through two large electrostatic precipitators, connected in parallel, where further removal of
particulate matter takes place. The gas stream then enters the sulphuric acid plants where
remaining fumes and dust are removed by wet scrubbing techniques and sulphur dioxide is
converted to sulphuric acid.
Dust and fume collected in the waste heat boiler and electrostatic precipitators are recycled to
the furnace charge for resmelting.
PR90579 Rev 0. 95/08                                                  PROOT. Rev. 3.96105
p:Aumunmin%90579doc%pr%90579.doc



2AIL                                                     Union Miniere - EIA of MDK Smelter
HATCH                                                                         Page 20
2.1.4 Conversion of Matte to Blister Copper
Copper matte tapped from the flash smelting furnace is transferred by ladle using overhead
travelling crane to the converter section. Conversion is carried out in one of three Peirce-Smith
converters each having the following design parameters:
Capacity             80-140 tonnes blister copper
Length               9.20 metres
Diameter            3.92 metres
Number of tuyeres    48
Matte is poured from the transfer ladles into the mouth of the converters; flux , scrap and revert
materials are charged in a similar manner. Air, enriched with oxygen, is blown through the
tuy&res which project through the side of the converter and are submerged in the melt. The
copper/iron matte, is oxidised in two stages by the air to form slag, blister copper metal, and
sulphur dioxide gas. Under typical operation one furnace is in the charging cycle whilst the
other is in the air blowing mode, so that the flow of SO2 laden gas from the converter section is
as constant as possible. The third converter is either under maintenance or on stand-by.
After completion of the air blow, the molten copper is poured from the converter into transfer
ladles. Copper is transferred by overhead crane to the anode furnaces.
Off-gases from the converter contain particulate matter and sulphur dioxide gas. The gas
stream is partially cooled in a waste heat boiler and then particulate matter is removed using two
Lurgi electrostatic precipitators connected in parallel. The gas stream then passes to the
sulphuric acid plant for final cleaning and SO2 removal.
2.1.5 Copper Refining and Anode Casting
The crude copper from the converter section is poured into one of two anode furnaces for
further refining. Each anode fumace has the following parameters:
Capacity        250 tonnes copper
Length           9.12 metre
Diameter        3.22 metre
Bumer           1200 kg/hour oil
In the anode furnace, the remaining sulphur in the copper is removed by air blowing, followed by
a reduction with oil.
The crude copper, which at this stage contains metallic impurities such as nickel, cobalt, arsenic
and precious metals is then cast into anodes on a casting wheel. The average weight of the
anodes is 250 kg each.
PRS0579 Rev. 0. 9sse                                                  PR.DOT. Rev. 3. 96,05
v nw,o m,905?79dcs r90S79 dcc



JI1Z                                                       Union Mini6re - EIA of MDK Smelter
HATCH                                                                           Page 21
2.1.6 Electrolytic Refining
Final refining of the copper is carried out in an electrorefinery with a capacity of 50 000 to 60 000
tonnes per year.  The copper refinery contains 766 electrolytic cells, through which an
electrolyte of copper sulphate/sulphuric acid is circulated. Copper anodes and cathode starter
sheets (thin sheets of pure copper) are placed in these cells. Electrical current passing through
the cells results in dissolution of copper from the anode and deposition of pure copper onto the
cathode. Some impurity elements in the anode, such as nickel, cobalt, iron and arsenic,
dissolve in the electrolyte whilst others which include precious metals are insoluble and remain
as a slime settled in the cells.
After a period of time in the cells, when the anode has corroded to a pre-required thickness, the
anodes and cathodes are removed. The cathodes are washed to remove entrained electrolyte
and then strapped into bundles for sale. The spent anodes are scrubbed to remove the
precious metal slime and then returned to the converter furnaces for remelting. Precious metal
slimes are treated for recovery of gold and silver.
2.1. 7 Precious Metals Refinery
Gold and silver are recovered from the anode slime using a hydrometallurgical process. The
slimes are first leached in hot sulphuric acid to dissolve copper. The leach residue containing
precious metals is roasted to volatilise selenium and then melted in an electric arc furnace. The
precious metal bullion is subject to fire refining before casting into anodes. Pure silver and gold
are recovered in a two stage electrorefining process.
2.1.8 Slag Treatment
The slag from the flash smelting furnace is transported in ladles by rail to the slag treatment
area. The slag is poured into pits and cooled by spraying with water. The cooled slag is then
broken up and treated in a benefication process which involves milling to a fine particle size
followed by a flotationiconcentration process to recover a copper concentrate.
The slag copper concentrate is retumed to the flash fumace charge; fayalite tailings from the
flotation process, which now contain less than 1 percent copper, are pumped to large settling
ponds. The fayalite consist mainly of iron oxides and iron silicates and other gangue materials.
Although some of the production is sold to the cement industry, the remainder is transported by
truck to a tailings pond at the Elatzite mine.
2.1.9 Sulphuric Acid
Sulphuric acid is produced from sulphur dioxide formed in the flash smelting and conversion
process. There are two Russian designed acid plants:
No. 1 is a single contact/ single absorption plant
- No. 2 is a double contact /double absorption plant
PR90579 Ret. 0. 95108                                                   PR.DOT, Rev. 3. 96)05
p:%unbomin90579W=% CxW 5793oc



Gi~L                                                       Union Miniere - EIA of MDK Smelter
HATCH                                                                           Page 22
Under normal operation all of the gas stream from the smelting furnace together with a portion of
the converter gas are treated in No 2 Acid Plant; the balance of the converter gas is treated in
No.1 Acid Plant.
The process of making sulphuric acid from sulphur dioxide involves the following sections:
a wet scrubbing section where the gas stream is cooled and remaining particulate matter
and metallic fumes are removed in spray towers. This is followed by further treatment in wet
electrostatic precipitators to remove sulphur trioxide.
- drying the saturated gas in a counter current flow of sulphuric acid
-  catalytic oxidation of sulphur dioxide to sulphur trioxide using vanadium pentoxide based
catalyst beds
absorption of sulphur trioxide from the gas stream by water in a circulating flow of sulphuric
acid
- removal of entrained sulphuric acid by mist eliminators prior to discharge of the tail gas to
the atmosphere via a stack
The produced sulphuric acid is pumped to a storage comprising eight, 5000 m3 tanks in a
bunded enclosure. There are facilities for loading sulphuric acid into rail tankers for dispatch to
customers.
A weak acid slurry produced in the gas cooling/cleaning section of the acid plant is treated to
neutralise the acid and remove arsenic and heavy metals.
2.1.10 Waste Water Cleaning
Waste acidic liquors arise from:
- the gas cooling/cleaning sections of the sulphuric acid plants
> the copper electrorefinery and the precious metals refinery where the accumulation of
impurities is controlled by bleeding a portion of the electrolytes from their respective circuits
Milk of lime produced in a lime slaking plant is added to the combined acid effluent to control the
pH at a value of about 11. Mixing is carried out by air sparging in a series of rectangular tanks.
The resultant slurry which contains a mixture of precipitated calcium sulphate, calcium arsenate
and heavy metal oxides is discharged to a large settling pond. Here the solids settle out and
the clarified liquor is decanted via an overflow weir into a channel which leads to the Pirdopska
River.
2.1.11 Services
2.1.11.1 Steam
The plant requirement for steam is provided by the waste heat boilers connected to the flash
smelting furnace and the converter furnaces. In winter months these are supplemented by five

oil fired boilers with a capacity of 12 000 kg/hour steam. In summer only one boiler is normally
in operation.
2.1.11.2 Oxygen
Oxygen with a purity of 96.5%, is produced by a fractional distillation technique using turbines to
compress the gas. The plant supplies compressed air as well as oxygen required for the
smelting and converting process. Some oxygen is also bottled for sale to customers.
2.2 Raw Materials
The main feed for the smelter consists of copper sulphided concentrates from domestic and
overseas sources. Local mines which supply concentrates include:
- Panagurski Medni Mini
- Medet
Ellatzite
- Majaravo
The total quantity of concentrates treated in 1996 was 454 483 tonnes.
Other feed materials include silica sand, and copper scrap. Limestone is used to prepare milk of
lime for neutralisation of acidic waste water.
2.3 Major Power Sources
The sources and approximate annual consumption of energy are:
electricity        210 000 Mwhr
heavy fuel oil     35 000 to 40 000 tonnes
-  propane            1 200 tonnes
The heavy oil, contains 3 to 3.5 % sulphur.
2.4 Water Supply and Consumption
Industrial Water is supplied by a 800mm diameter pipeline from the Dushantzi reservoir, which is
approximately 12 km north of the MDK site. The dam is owned and maintained by MDK. The
maximum water demand is about 400 litres per second (1440 m3 per hour)and is used as
process water and as cooling water for the smelter, acid plant and oxygen plant.
Potable water is supplied from another small reservoir fed by the rivers Slavska and Sanar Dere.
The average usage is 1411 m3 per day.
Water for make-up in the waste heat and utility boilers is demineralised in an ion exchange unit
having a maximum capacity of 80 cubic metres per hour.

2.8 Storage Facilities
2.8.1 Concentrates
Facilities for storage of concentrates include:
- 3 open storage areas of 5000 square metres each
- 2 inside areas of 1500 and 2000 square metres
Other inside storage areas are used for storing finished product, intermediate materials and
maintenance spares.
2.8.2 Sulphuric Acid
Product sulphuric acid is stored in eight tanks each having a capacity of 5000 cubic metres. The
tanks are installed in a bunded enclosure which has sufficient capacity to contain the contents of
one full tank in the event of failure. The bund sump is equipped with level alarms to wam of
leaks and a pump to transfer leaks or spillage to the reserve tank which is always empty.
There are facilities to load rail tankers at the storage area.
2.8.3 Fuel
Fuel oil is stored in two tanks of 5000 cubic meters each; the tanks are surrounded by a bund to
contain leaks.
Propane is stored in 2 underground tanks each having a capacity of 100 cubic metres.
2.9 Efficiency of Power Use
Electricity is supplied from the national grid through two supply channels. The total consumption
in 1995 was 208 660 kWhr which is about 40% of the available supply.

3. REVIEW OF ENVIRONMENTAL MANAGEMENT
3.1 Structure of Environmental Management
The environmental management in MDK is a responsibility of the managing director. Reporting
directly to him is the manager of the Environmental Protection department.
This department is sub-divided into two sectors; air protection, and water and soil protection.
Each sector includes a laboratory and is led by a specialist. The Technical and emissions gas
analysis laboratory is managed by a chemist and employs 6 technicians. The Water and solid
products' analysis laboratory is managed by the water and solid products' specialist, and has a
personnel of 3.
3.2 Legislative Requirements for Environmental Protection
3.2.1 Standards for Air Emissions
According to "Norms for permissible emissions/concentrations in flue gases/of impurities outlet
in atmosphere" issued by The Ministry of Environment/State Gazette No. 8 dd 19911, the legal
requirements for air protection are as follows:
For impurities' emissions generated by the organised sources of pollution:
- 325 meter stack for gases from the smelter and boilers
dust     max. 20 mg/M3
Sox      max. 800 mg/m3
- 120meter stack for gases from thre sulphuric acid plant
S02      max. 2.6 kg/ton of produced 100% sulphuric acid (733 mg/m3)
S03      max. 0.6 kg/ton of produced 100% sulphuric acid (max. 150 mg/m3)
S03      max. 120 mg/mr3
3.2.2 Standards For Water Protection
According to Instruction No. 7 for norms determining the quality of flowing surface waters,
issued by The Ministry of Environment / State Gazette No 96/1986), the norms for water
protection are as follows:
For effluents out of the slime pond and into the category If river of Pirdopska and category IlIl
rivers of Sanar Dere and Kisselo Dere:

3.3 Permits, Licences and Decisions on EIA
MDK has not obtained any permits or licences. An Environmental Impact Assessment has been
prepared but a decision from the Ministry of Environment Supreme Experts' Council has been
postponed pending the issue of the new EIA by UM/Hatch.

3.5 Investments for Environmental Protection
MDK's environmental investments for 1996 totals 100 million leva. It is impossible to quote
precise figures on these types of investments for the period 1990-1995 due to prevailing
inflationary processes.
3.6 Annual Expenses for Environmental Protection
Environmental expenses include all requirements for repair and maintenance of the
environmental protection facilities, environmental monitoring equipment, as well as all pre-
feasibility studies concerning environmental protection. The relevant facilities are:
sulphuric acid plants and the 2.ESPs for de-dusting after the drying furnaces are registered
as final air treatment facilities
-  the lime neutralisation effluents' treatment station, the small lagoons, slime pond, and the
sanitary waters treatment station are all considered as waste waters' treatment facilities.
It is not possible to provide a breakdown of the costs associated with environmental protection.
3.7 Public Complaints Concerning Operation of the Facility
The communities of Pirdop and Zlatitsa have not filed any complaints, staged protests, or sent
signals for blast pollution resulting from the entity's operation since 1995.
Data on the health status of the area's population is provided in Section 4.1 and 5.1.

3.8 Industrial Emergencies and Response Plans
The following major environmental incidents have been reported:
- June 28th, 1988. As a result of failure of the absorber in the No 2 sulphuric acid plant, a fog
of sulphuric acid was emitted from the 120-metre stack for 10 minutes. The emission fell on
the town of Zlatitsa. Children from the northern part of Zlatitsa had to be evacuated. There
were no casualties.
- The town councils of Pirdop and Zlatitsa submitted an official letter saying there was
increased gaseous emissions from MDK just prior to the plant's shutdown in June 1994
April, 1992. Following a leakage of mono-hydrate sulphuric acid from the old reservoir, the
river of Topolnitza was polluted as a result of which all fish were dead, all the way up to the
inlet of the Topolnitza dam.
May, 1994. While decanting a sulphuric acid tank, the river of Topolnitza was once again
polluted, although this time the fish were not affected.
MDK has developed emergency action plans for all situations that could damage elements of
the environment. These plans were drafted jointly by the"Citizens' Protection Service" and the
environmental department.
In 1997 the plan 'Instruction for air protection from ovemorm pollution with impurities resulting
from MDK's operating activity" was implemented and commissioned. The instruction deals with
technology and emergency occurrences which could generate blast ejections of impurities into
the atmosphere, as well as procedures for staff to follow in order to eliminate the repercussions.

4.       CHARACTERISTICS   OF   POLLUTANTS   AND   ASSESSMENT   OF
CONSEQUENCES FOR THE ENVIRONMENT
4.1 Air
MDK operates a complex technological process for recovering copper and by-products from a
variety of copper ores and concentrates. The combination of the processes involved, and the
potentially harmful nature of the materials treated, results in the release of gaseous and
particulate material into the atmosphere. Management and control of these releases is therefore
a high priority so that operation of the facility does not lead to environmental damage of the
ecosystem.
In order to assess the potential impact of atmospheric emissions from MDK it is necessary to
understand:
- the current state of the air and meteorological conditions which influence the impact of
releases
- the sources of emissions to air from MDK
- the nature of control systems employed to prevent or minimise release to air, their suitability
and effectiveness
- the methods used for monitoring emissions inside and outside the plant
the Bulgarian environmental legislation regulations that MDK must conform to and how these
regulations compare to standards and guidelines used in other jurisdictions or those
recommended by international investment banks
4.1.1 Climate and Meteorological Data
The Zlatitsa-Pirdop valley lies within-the European continental climatic zone. However the local
climate is influenced by its geographical setting in a deep valley within two mountain ranges.
The area is characterised by milder winters and cooler summers than other areas in Bulgaria.
4.1.1.1 Temperature
Average monthly temperatures in the Pirdop Zlatitsa region are given in Table 4.1.1-1.

The interaction of geological factors and meteorological conditions gives rise to the temperature
inversion phenomena for a large proportion of the year. Frequency and continuity of periods of
close to land inversion are shown in Table 4.1.1-3.

4.1.1.4 Rainfall
The area is typified by rainfall throughout the year with an annual average of 617mm. However,
the proportion of rainfall in the summer months from May to August is greater than the rest of the
year, with an average of 300mm.
Snow cover in the region lasts from 60 to 80 days, primarily in the months of January and
February, and reaches depths of 700mm
4.1.1.5 Air Quality in the Region of MDK(
The characteristics of the local meteoroligical conditions are important to the impact of
emissions from MDK on the region. Of particular relevance are:
- the prevailing directions of the wind which direct the emissions towards the local urban
centres
- the prolonged periods of calm weather which prevents efficient dispersal of emissions
In addition the location within the valley surrounded by mountains leads to temperature inversion
phenomena and air recirculation effects which also hinder dispersal of emissions.
The location of Pirdop and Zlatitsa relative to the MDK site together with the prevailant weather
conditions make them susceptable to emissions from the plant. Ambient air monitoring in Pirdop
and Zlatitsa indicate that the concentrations of sulphur dioxide, sulphuric acid aerosols, dust and
hydogen sulphide in the atmosphere have frequently exceeded the accceptable standards
according to Bulgarian law. This is discussed further in Section 4.1.3.

4.1.2 Sources of Emissions
Air emissions from MDK are classified into the following groups:
- controlled emissions of process gas
- uncontrolled emissions of process gas
- fugitive emissions
Controlled emissions comprise process off-gas streams which are subject to some form of
abatement prior to discharge to atmosphere via either the 325 m stack or the 120 m stack.
Uncontrolled air emissions comprise process emissions that are captured at source but then
discharged to atmosphere without any prior cleaning or treatment via the high stacks.
Emissions that result from process operations, material handling and material storage that are
released directly to the atmosphere without any prior cleaning or treatment are defined as
fugitive emissions.
A simplified flowsheet showing the sources and routes of air emissions from the processes is
given in Figure 4.1-2. Descriptions and characteristics of each point source and the control
techniques used to minimise release of pollutants are given in the following sections.
4.1.2.1 Controlled Emissions
Controlled emissions consist of the treated process gas streams from the following operations:
- flash smelting fumace
- converter furnace
- concentrate dryer
Brief descriptions of these units and their operations are provided in a previous chapter.
4.1.2.1.1 Flash Smelting Furnace
Off-gas generated by the flash smelting process contains suspended particulate matter,
metallurgical fume and gaseous substances, in particular sulphur dioxide. A typical flow rate
and off-gas composition for the flash furnace operating at a concentrate flow rate of 60 to 80 tph
is:
Volume Flow Rate     60,000   Nm3/hr
Sulphur dioxide, SO2   15 - 20   %vol
Sulphur trioxide, S03   0.5 -2.0  %vol
Oxygen, 02           1.2      %vol
Carbon dioxide       6,5      %vol
Dust                 70 -150  mg/Nm3

4. 1.2.1.2 Converting furnace
Off-gas from the converter furnace is collected in water cooled hood arrangements and carried
in ducts to the common inlet plenum of a waste heat boiler.  Typical characteristics of the
converter gas are:
Volume Flow Rate      60,000  Nm 3/hr
Dust                    15    mg/Nm3
Sulphur dioxide, SO2    5 - 8   %vol
4.1.2.1.3 Concentrate Dryer
The off-gas from the concentrate dryer contains particulate matter and products of oil
combustion. Sulphur dioxide is present due to the high sulphur content (:3%) of the oil. For the
dryer operating at a concentrate feed rate of 60 to 80 tonnes per hour, the typical characteristics
of the off-gas are:
Volume Flow Rate       70 000    Nm3/hr
Dust                  50 - 300   mg/Nm3
Sulphur dioxide, SO2    0.2 -0.6   %vol
4.1.2.2 Uncontrolled Air Emissions
The following uncontrolled emission sources have been identified at MDK:
- Flash Fumace Matte and Slag Tapping
Emissions generated from matte and slag tapping operations are collected in a
secondary fume system consisting of a local tap hole hood, covered launder and ladle
hood. Once collected, the fume is vented to the atmosphere via the 325 m stack. This
fume contains low levels of SO2 and dust, and is well below the specified emission limits.
- Peirce-Smith Converter Charging and Tapping
The converters are equipped with secondary hoods to capture charging and tapping
emissions. However, these hoods provide no apparent fume control due to inadequate
exhaust flow rate. Any fume collected by the secondary hoods is exhausted to the
atmosphere via the 325 m stack.
- Anode Furnace Emissions
Emissions from the anode furnace are vented directly to the atmosphere via the 325 m
stack. The composition and flow rate of the off-gas stream will vary significantly over the
complete cycle of the furnace (oxidation, reduction, and heating). The S02 concentration
in the off-gas can be significant during the oxidation cycle. There are facilities to recycle
a portion of the anode fumace gas stream to the dryers, but are not in regular use.
PR9S579 Rev. 0. 95OS                                                  PR.OT, Rev. 3. 96S05
p:%unKnmrnk9O579979. aoc



ZLL                                                      JUnion Mini&re - EIA of MDK Smelter

Utility Steam Boilers
Combustion emissions from the oil fired boilers are vented directly to the atmosphere via
the 325 m stack. Typically two boilers producing 12.5 tph steam at 0.6 MPa pressure
are fired in the winter months only. Sulphur dioxide will be present in the off-gas stream
due to the high sulphur content of the oil. The typical gas volume and composition from
one boiler are:
Volume         13 500   Nm3/hr
SO2            3000      mgl Nm3
NO2            318       mg/ Nm3
> Flash Fumace Emergency Stack
During abnormal furnace operating conditions it may be required to vent gases directly to
the atmosphere for a short time period via a local stack at the furnace area. Any gas
released during this period will be high in sulphur oxides, and will have a significant
impact on the local ambient air quality.
4.1.2.3 Fugitive Emissions
The impact of fugitive emissions on ambient air quality can be highly significant because of the
height at which they are released and the relatively slow rate of dispersion. The following major
fugitive emission sources have been identified at MDK:
- Flash Fumace Waste Heat Boiler
Leakage of flash furnace off-gas from the waste heat boiler directly to the atmosphere is
estimated to be the largest single source of fugitive emissions from furnace operations.
The SO2 and dust content of the off-gas will be high.
- Flash Furnace Reaction Shaft
Leakage of gas from the reaction shaft is emitted into the smelter and is released into the
atmosphere primarily through the ladle aisle roof vent. The flow rate of this emission
source is estimated to be 20 000 to 30 000 m3/hr, and will contain a high percentage of
S02 gas. Considerable dilution will occur prior to being released to the atmosphere.
- Peirce-Smith Converter Charging and Tapping
Emissions generated during charging and tapping operations are released into the ladle
aisle and vented from the smelter building through the roof vent. These emissions are
released on an intermittent basis that typically lasts from 2 to 4 minutes, with the number
of emission periods per day dependent on the production rate. The flow rate at the roof
vent is estimated to be between 150 000 to 250 000 m3/hr, and the principal pollutants
will be dust and S02.
PR90579 Rev. 0. 95108                                                PR.OT. Rev. 3. 961
p:\un,Aw C9S?079tdocs%P.90579 doC



19ik                                                      Union Miniere - EIA of MDK Smelter
HATCH                                                                         Page 39
.- Peirce-Smith Converter Slag Transfer to Slag Pots
Emissions released during transfer of converter slag from the ladle to the slag pots are
released directly into the smelter and are vented to the atmosphere via the ladle aisle
roof vent. The quantity of S02 gas and dust in this emission stream is small.
- Anode Furnace Fugitive Emissions
Anode furnace fugitive emissions arise from charging and tapping operations, plus
puffing of gases from the furnace mouth during reduction, oxidation and holding periods.
The contribution of this emission source to the overall smelter emission release rate is
small.
- Smelter Slag Pile
Small quantities of dust and fume are released from the slag pile in the smelter when
lades are emptied and when the slag is being loaded into charge boats with a front end
loader. The overall contribution to the total smelter emission rate is small. The potential
for hydrogen suiphide production exists during water cooling of the slag pile.
= Material Handling
Airbome dust emissions are generated during the handling, transport, and mixing of dry
materials in open areas.  These fugitive emission sources cannot be accurately
quantified.
- Waste Material Storage
Open storage areas for tailings produced at the flotation plant and calcium arsenate
residues are a source of wind borne dust emissions.
4.1.3 Facilities for Control and Monitoring
4.1.3.1 Flash Fumace Off-Gas Control
An induced draft fan extracts the off-gas from the fumace via an uptake duct and into a complex
system designed to cool and clean the gas prior to release into the atmosphere. The gas
handling system consists of:
- A waste heat boiler for gas cooling and preliminary dust collection
- Two hot gas electrostatic precipitators operating in parallel for removal of particulate matter
-  A double contact, double absorption and a single contact, single absorption sulphuric acid
plants that convert sulphur dioxide into concentrated sulphuric acid.  The acid plants
comprise several discreet stages:
PR90579 Rev. 0. 9508                                                  PRMOOT. R-. 3. 96M5
p:un*n0n\9da9s794wp90s79 doc



Union Miniere - EIA of MOK Smelter
HATCH                                                                         Page 40
D a wet scrubbing process to quench and clean the gas
>  a wet electrostatic precipitator to remove sulphur dioxide and final traces of fume
>  a counter current drying stage for the sulphur dioxide rich gas stream
> the contact section where SO2 is oxidised to S03 using vanadium pentoxide based
catalysts
>  the absorption stage where S03 is absorbed by sulphuric acid
A demister unit where fine acid droplets are removed prior to discharge via the 120 metre
stack
Under the present operating regime, the double contact, double absorption acid plant treats all
off-gas until it reaches capacity. Surplus gas is treated in the single contact, single absorption
acid plant.
The design parameters of the major items of control equipment are:
Waste heat boiler:
Flow capacity                140,000 Nm3/hr
Gas inlet temperature        1350 °C
Gas outlet temperature      400 °C
Electrostatic precipitator (each):
Flow capacity                65,000 Nm3/hr
Inlet dust loading          70 to 120 g/Nm3
Outlet dust concentration    150 mg/ Nm3
Cleaning efficiency         99.875 %
Double contact acid plant:
Flow capacity               60,000 to 130,000 Nm3/hr
Inlet S02                   7.0 to 9.0%vol.
Conversion efficiency        99.7%
Combined stack mist and SO3  75 mg/Nm3
Single contact acid plant:
Flow capacity               60,000 to 125,000 Nm3/hr
Inlet S02                   3.0 to 6.5%vol.
Conversion efficiency       97%
4.1.3.2 Converter Off-Gas Control
The converter off-gas is treated in a similar manner to the flash fumace off-gas. That is, after
passing through a waste heat boiler and two electrostatic precipitators operating in parallel, the
converter off-gas is combined with the flash fumace off-gas, treated in the acid plant, and
discharged via the 120m stack.

Flow capacity                75,000 Nm3/hr

4.1 3.3 Concentrate Dryer Off-Gas Control
Two Lurgi electrostatic precipitators are installed (one for each dryer) to capture the dust prior to
discharge via the 325 metre stack. There is no control of the sulphur dioxide component in the
gas stream.
The design parameters for each of the electrostatic precipitators are:
Flow capacity                72,000 Nm3/hr
Inlet dust loading          50 to 200 g/Nm3
Outlet dust concentration    150 mg/Nm3
Cleaning efficiency         99.5 %
4.1.3.4 Status of Emission Control Equipment
Flash Fumace Precipitators
The furnace is currently operating at a feed rate considerably lower than the design capacity.
One consequence of this is that the off-gas volume is approximately 50 percent of the design
rating of the downstream gas handling system. The problems associated with this lower than
design flow include:
long residence time in the boiler
- overcooling of the gas
sulphur trioxide formation due to catalytic oxidation of sulphur trioxide
Sulphur trioxide concentrations in the boiler exit gas have been measured recently at 2 to 3
percent. Such levels together with overcooling of the gas stream create serious problems in the
electrostatic precipitator due to acid condensation and formation of sulphate deposits on the
electrode surfaces. This leads to loss of cleaning efficiency and exit gases containing several
grammes per cubic metre of dust compared with the design expectation of SOmgm3. Although
the dust is subsequently captured in the sulphuric acid plant the additional dust loading and high
sulphur trioxide concentration increases the volume of weak acid produced and hence the
quantity of neutralised sludge for disposal.
PR90579 Rov. 0. 9510e                                                 PFOOT, Rev. 3. 96105
p: nmm's057sn79Wacs Vt9S5T9.doc



Union Miniere - EIA of MDK Smelter
HATCH                                                                         Page 42
One of the precipitators has recently been substantially modified in an attempt to alleviate these
problems. The modification involved conversion from 3 to 5 fields and the installation of new
electrodes. However it will not been possible to assess the impact of these modifications until
the plant has run consistently following the recent maintenance shutdown.
Converter Fumace Precipitators
The dust removal efficiency of the electrostatic precipitators associated with the Peirce Smith
converters is also less than the design rating due to similar reasons explained above albeit to a
lesser extent.
Concentrate Dryer Precipitators
Typical dust concentrations in the precipitator exit gas are several hundred mg per cubic metre
compared with a design rating of 150 mg/im3 and an emission requirement of only 20mg/m3.
Although the preipitators are not achieving the design cleaning efficiency even under optimum
conditions they are unable to meet the requirements of environmental legislation.
Sulphuric Acid Plants
Measurements at MDK indicate that the double contact acid plant is operating with an efficiency
near to the design and that the sulphur dioxide concentration in the tail gas is less than the
required standard.
The single contact plant is unable to meet the emission criteria and the sulphur dioxide level in
the tail gas exceeds the standards by a factor of approximately six.
The current operating regime of the smelter results in a low grade of matte produced. Thus the
blowing time required in the converters is longer than ideal for correct sequencing. It is normal
smelter operating practice to operate with only one converter blowing at a time. However it is
understood that occasionally two converters are in the blowing cycle simultaneously. Under
these circumstances the combined gas volume capacity of the acid plants is insufficient to
handle the total gas flow from the smelter and the converters.
The average tail gas compositions from the acid plants in 1995 and 1996 were:
Nol Plant             No 2 Plant
%Vol                  %Vol
S02         0.17                   0.049
S03         0.0068                 0.0047
4.1.3.5 Monitoring
Routine monitoring of gas streams is carried out to assist process control and to check stack
emissions for compliance with regulatory standards.
PR90579 Rev. O. 9518                                                  PR DOT, Rev. 3. 96105
p:imonmw t90579kdOCsp3r0S79.doc



2i~L                                                    Union Miniere - EIA of MDK Smelter
HATCH                                                                       Page 43
Stack Emissions
The tail gases from each acid plant and the combined gas stream entering the 120 metre stack
are monitored continuously for sulphur dioxide using Hartmann Braun instrumentation. Results
are plotted continuously on chart recorders in the acid plant control room and also logged by
computer which is programmed to interpret the results as instantaneous, hourly or 24 hourly
averages. These measurements are supplemented by chemical analyses carried out three
times per day on each acid plant tail gas and on the inlet gases so that the conversion
efficiencies can be monitored.
Gas compositions of the furnace precipitator outlet gas are measured several times each day
to assist process control in the furnace as well as the acid plants.
The combined gas stream from uncontrolled emissions prior to entering the 325 metre stack is
monitored for dust, suphur dioxide and nitrogen oxides twice per week.
Ambient Air Quality
Monitoring of the sulphur dioxide concentration in the ambient air is carried out by automatic
monitoring stations located in the towns of Pirdop and Zlatitsa. The instruments, which use a
UV fluorescence technique, are owned by MOK. The average concentration is measured over
15 minute periods and the results are transmitted directly to the computer centre at MDK where
they can be presented as 30 minute or 24 hour and monthy averages.

- 0.3035 mg/m3 sulphur dioxide at a distance of 1906 metres
- 0.009 mg/m3 dust at a distance of 2141 metres

under certain weather conditions. This was witnessed during the plant assessment when the
plume from the stack was observed to be falling towards ground level close to the plant

quality measurements carried out during the assessment revealed the following:

These are above above the maximum acceptable limits for ambient air.
4.1.4.3 Ambient Air Quality in Pirdop and Zlatitsa
The pollution of air by dust and sulphur dioxide in Pirdop and Zlatitsa is caused by emissions
from:
> industrial sources
- domestic fuel burning
- vehicles
Domestic heating is a significant source of sulphur dioxide in winter months.  However,
monitoring data suggests that MDK is the principal emitter of sulphur dioxide and particulate
matter, as will be demonstrated by the following results.
Emissions from the smelter and wind blown dust from dry storage areas are difficult to quantify.
An indirect assessment of these emissions can be gained by measurement of dust and gaseous
substances in the atmosphere in the region of MDK.
Air quality in the Pirdop-Zlatitsa region is monitored at four sites. Two are located in Zlatitsa,
approximately 1500 metres west of MDK; and two are located in Pirdop, approximately 1500
metres north east of MDK. Up until 1994 there was only one monitoring station in each town.
The results of the air monitoring for the last 10 years are given in the Interstitial Report,
Environmental Assessment of the Impact of Emissions of MDK Pirdop on soils in the Region
Pirdop-Zlatitsa, Section 4. Annual average concentrations of dust, sulphur dioxide, sulphuric
acid and arsenic for the last 10 years are presented graphically and are included in the
Technical Support Document - Air Emission. The annual average concentration of hydrogen
sulphide in the period 1992 to 1996 is also presented in this document.
The results of the air monitoring programme in comparison with Bulgarian criteria are
summarised below:
Dust
Between 1986 and 1996 the average dust concentration was frequently in excess of the
maximum acceptable concentration (MAC) in both Pirdop and Zlatitsa. The highest recorded
concentrations were in 1990 and 1991 when the annual concentrations exceeded the MAC by a
factor of approximately 3. Since this period there appears to have been a general decrease in
dust levels. In 1995 and 1996 one station in Pirdop recorded values slightly in excess of MAC
PR90579 Rev. 0. 95W                                                 PRPDOT. Re. 3. 9605
p:%unnSnW579W=WV790579 doc



Union Miniere - EIA of MDK Smelter
HATCH                                                                     Page 46
whereas the other was approximately half of the MAC. A similar pattern was observed in
Zlatitsa in this period.
Sulphur Dioxide
The annual average sulphur dioxide concentrations in Pirdop and Zlatitsa were significantly
above the MAC in the period 1986 to 1996. The highest recorded concentration was in 1991
when the level in Zlatitsa was more than 10 times the MAC; in Pirdop in the same period the
average concentration of sulphur dioxide was more than 8 times the MAC. In preceding years
the concentrations were between 6 and 8 times the MAC. The high values in 1991 were
attributed to the intermittent operation of the fumace and frequent venting of fumace off-gas via
the emergency stack.
Since 1993 there appears to have been a general decline in the annual average sulphur dioxide
concentrations both in Pirdop and Zlatitsa. In 1996 the annual average concentrations in
Zlatitsa were approximately 0.1 mg/m3 and 0.06 mg/m3 (compared with a MAC value of 0.05). In
Pirdop one monitoring station recorded levels slightly above the MAC whereas the second
station was approximately half the MAC. However, 24 hour average concentrations for all four
stations were exceeded frequently in 1996; the highest instantaneous level being 14 times
above the MAC. The standards were exceeded on 23% of the days. The results for the period
1992 to 1996 are given in Table 4.1.4-3.

The annual distribution of ambient sulphur dioxide concentration shows peak concentrations
during the winter months of January to March. Minimum values are associated with the summer
period. The high average sulphur dioxide levels in winter are attributable partly to burning of
high sulphur fuels in the urban areas of Pirdop and Zlatitsa. The very low levels recorded during
June and July coincide with MDK's annual shutdown.
Sulphuric Acid Aerosol
This parameter was measured at two stations, one in Pirdop and one in Zlatitsa, for the period
1986 to 1996. The average annual concentrations measured over this period were reasonably
consistent between 0.3 and 0.5 mg/im3. However, in 1994 the annual average rose to above 0.8
mg/im3 at the Zlatitsa station.
Hydrogen Sulphide
In general there has been a steady decline in hydrogen sulphide at both monitoring locations
since 1992. In 1996 the hydrogen sulphide concentration was approximately 1.5 times the MAC
of 0.008 mg/mr3 compared with 16 times greater than the MAC in 1992. Measurements during
the course of 1996 do not indicate any significant seasonal variations, although the annual peak
was recorded on August coinciding with increased production at MDK.
Nitrogen Dioxide
Measurements in Pirdop and Zlatitsa show that annual average values of nitrogen dioxide are
well below the MAC value. Furthermore, there were no instances of short term (30 minute)
concentrations recorded.
Arsenic
Ambient arsenic concentrations were measured at Pirdop and Zlatitsa in the period 1986 to
1996. The highest annual averages were recorded in 1995 and 1996 at about 0.0016 mg/m3 in
Zlatitsa.
4.1.4.4 Impact Assessment
The air monitoring programme carried out during the last decade has revealed that average
levels of suspended particulate matter and sulphur dioxide are in excess of the maximum
acceptable concentration permitted by Bulgarian environmental legislation. The concentrations
of these substances have also exceeded European Union (EU), World Health Organisation
(WHO) and US EPA limit or guideline values. These are compared with Bulgarian standards in
Tables 4.1.4-4 and for dust and Table 4.1.4-5 for sulphur dioxide.  In general, Bulgarian
standards are compatible with limit and guideline values recommended by these organisations.

To assess the state of health df-the population in the area around the MDK and of the
employees in the plant itself, data from the municipal hospital and the environmental impact
assessment studies were used. The annual reports of the Ministry of Health were used to
obtain morbidity in the country as a whole. The following is a summary of the findings from
source reference (2).
The inhabitants of Pirdop, Zlatitsa and Chelophech, as well as the employees, are exposed to
direct, indirect and cumulative impact of the emissions from processes in the plant.
Measurements for pollution of air, soil and water prove the presence of toxic chemical
compounds and dust in quantities exceeding the permissible concentrations. The last extensive
study carried out by the NCHMEH in 1990 showed increase of the arsenic content in the nails,
hairs and urine of the examined adults and children. The levels of cadmium and, in a lesser
degree, of lead and zinc in their blood were also shown to be increased. A change in white
blood cells count (eosiinophilia) was found in the blood of children suggesting possible allergic
diseases. The prescence of these substances has the potential to impact the health of the
population.

In view of the environmental pollution recorded it was anticipated that higher morbidity would
occur in the region. Examination of patients over 15 years of age during the 1992-1996 period
showed the incidence of common diseases, of the population, as listed in Table 4.1.4-7, to be
similar or somewhat smaller that the population of the whole community.
According to data from the municipality hospital, a higher incidence of tuberculosis, malignant
neoplasms, pulmonary and renal diseases is estimated. Lung diseases in the region of Pirdop,
Zlatitsa and Chelopech are less than the mean for the country. The reason is that acute
respiratory diseases in the country as a whole are higher than in the region due to epidemic
"bursts" of influenza and other acute viral respiratory diseases. The closeness of the region to
Sofia, where major national hospitals and health-care centres are found, allows patients to be
offered qualified medical service. In spite of their overall lesser incidence as compared to the
mean for the country, some respiratory diseases tend to become more frequent in the region.

4.2 Waters
4.2.1 Characteristics of Surface Water
The main region under the influence of environmental emissions form the MDK smelter is the
Pirdop-Ziatitsa valley. The valley consists of arable farms including pastures and meadows with
a totai land area of 8500 ha. Major centres of population are at the villages of Pirdop, Zlatitsa,
Mirkavo, Anton and Chelopech. The area under assessment contains part of an important
mining industry that includes ore mining, ore beneficiation and metallurgical processes. These
industries play an important role in the economy of the region, but are also the main source of
industrial pollution in the Pirdop-Zlatitsa valley.
The combination of geological setting, climatic effects and proximity to the site is highly
significant with respect to the environmental impact of water emissions from MDK on the Pirdop
Zlatitsa region.
4.2. 1. 1 Surface Water System and Groundwater Hydrology
The Zlatitsa-Pirdop area is situated in the catchment area of the Topolnitza river, which rises in
the Sredna Gora range and flows southwards towards the Plovdiv plain. Major tributaries which
influx the Topolnitza are the Pirdopska, Zlatishka and Chelopechka which arise in the southem
slopes of the Balkan mountain range. The surface water system is illustrated on Figure 1.3-2
and the schematic map of local streams is Figure 4.2-1.
The Zlatishka and Pirdopska rivers are of particular relevance since they receive waste waters
from the MDK site and then flow through the towns Pirdop and Zlatitsa. These two rivers run
east and west of the MDK plant, respectively. At the plant, the rivers are fed by surface waters
of water catch basins with areas of 39 km2 and 73 kM2, respectively. After the Zlatishka and
Pirdopska the rivers Chlopechka, Tzarkvenishka, Selska and Mirkovska join the Topolnitza
River. The flow of the Topolnitza is regulated by a dam south of the town of Porbrene. The dam
provides a reservoir of water which is used for irrigation in the important agricultural region of the
Plovdiv plain.
The slope of the natural ground ranges from 3 to 5% in the plant and is underlain by alluvial soils
consisting of mixtures of clays, silt, sands and gravels. These soils have high clay content at
near the surface and become more silty and sandy with depth. The groundwater was observed
to be 8 to 10 m below the ground surface with the surface clay providing a cap over the
groundwater.

4.2.1.2 Water Resources, Quantitative and Qualitative Characteristics
Rain water and relatively neutral MDK plant wastewater are collected in any one of three
streams and are discharged untreated into either the Pirdopska or the Zlatitsa Rivers. Drain
water from the eastern 50 hectares and surface water from the slag treatment process and slag
storage areas flow into the Kiselo Dere stream at an average flow rate of 40 litres/second (I/s).
Drainage from the western part of the plant, which includes the production areas, flows into the
Sanar Dere channel at a flow rate of 110 I/s. The Kiselo Dere empties into the Pirdopska River
while the Sanar Dere feeds the Zlatishka River. The pH and the concentrations of sulphate,
electrical conductivity and heavy metals such as copper and arsenic in the Sanar Dere, Kiselo
Dere and the slime lagoon channel that decants water from the acid plant effluent treatment
plant that flow into the Pirdopska River frequently exceed Bulgarian Regulation No. 7 standards.
These streams also contain a substantial concentration of suspended solids while the drainage
water flowing into the Sanar Dere has been found to be contaminated with oil. Sanctions have
been applied against MDK for continuously exceeding the environmental standards established
by the govemment.
According to a study made by the Bulgarian Ministry of the Environment and Water in 1995,
MDK used 12,065,000 cubic metres of water from the Dushantzi Dam for production purposes
(i.e. an average of 380 IVs). Another 51,200 cubic metres (i.e. average 1.6 Is) were extracted
from the Slavtzi and Sanar Dere for drinking water. Although there is no reported shortage of
drinking water supply at present, water usage by MDK is an issue that must be considered since
adequate clean water supply for drinking and irrigation represents a potential future problem for
the region.  The drinking water supply network and treatment plants are in need of
modemization. Some towns, such as Zlatitsa, do not have waste water treatment facilities.
As the main consumer of water, MDK should consider implementation of intermediate and long-
term measures to control and optimize water usage by adopting improved water treatment
methods, as well as reuse and recycling of water wherever technically and economically
feasible. This will result in an increased supply of clean drinking water for the towns of Pirdop
and Zlatitza. In addition to this, the banks of the rivers Slavska and Koru Dere must be
reinforced to prevent further erosion, which greatly contributes to the total dissolved and
suspended solids in the rivers.
The flow characteristics of the Topolnitza and its tributaries vary considerably according to the
season. Furthermore the flow of the Topolnitza is regulated by the Dushantzi dam, which is
used to supply "industrial water" to MDK. The mean perennial water discharge of the Topolnitza
and the relevant tributaries are given in Table 4.2.-1.
The peak flow of the rivers occurs in March and April and the lowest in August and September.
Liquid effluent discharges from MDK and the towns of Zlatitsa and Pirdop guarantees minimum
flow of 100 - 150 I/s and 50 - 60 I/s for the Pirdopska and Zlatishka respectively. The average
relative contributions of these tributaries to the total flow of the Topolnitza are 12 to 15 percent

on the water quality of the Topolnitza river.

4.2.2 Sources of Waste Water Discharge from MDK
There are three primary waste effluent streams discharging from MDK. These are sanitary
waste water, surface drainage water, and liquid effluent from the neutralization plant. The
sanitary waste stream is discharged into the Pirdopka River upon being biologically treated and
neutralized if need be. At present, the drainage water flows untreated into the Kiselo Dere or
Sanar Dere, which empty into the Pirdopska and Zlatishka Rivers, respectively.  These
untreated effluent streams have been found to contain large concentrations of suspended solids
and hazardous wastes, such as sulphates and arsenic. Many of these pollutants are picked up
from the slag treatment and storage areas.

A survey of all key water users and water discharge points was conducted at the MDK site
during the period July 23-26, 1997. This period was selected for sampling after the smelter and
acid plants were restarted on July 8-10, 1997, allowing about 2 weeks for the process to
stabilize and the concentrations in the various water streams to become more representative of
steady state. During this 3-day sampling and monitoring period, composite water samples were
taken from each of the twenty four sample points three times per day at 6-hour intervals. The
composite sampling from each of-the 24 points was repeated on the following day to allow not
only for shift-to-shift variability but also for day-to-day variability in the flow and concentrations.
The samples from each day were blended for each location and analysed separately for each of
the two days at an MEW - approved laboratory in Sofia, as well as at MDK's laboratory.
Furthermore, single 2-day composite samples from each sample point were also prepared and
analysed in a Canadian ISO - certified environmental laboratory. The following acidic effluents
were audited:
4.2.2.1 Acidic Process Effluents
A primary environmental concern at MDK is the arsenic laden 'weak acid" Process Effluent
which combines the following acidic streams (see Appendix B - Figure 6.2.2.1):
- Acid plant No. 1 quench tower effluent
- Acid plant No. 1 wet electrostatic precipitator (ESP) effluent
- Acid plant No. 2 quench tower effluent
- Acid plant No. 2 wet ESP effluent
PR90579 Rev. 0. 95O                                                   PRMOOT. Rev. 3, 96t5
D W ,J-n%9O579%doc3%,rvr9O579 Oc



Glr_U~I                                                  Union Miniere - EIA of MDK Smelter
HATCH                                                                         Page 57
- Acidic effluent (i.e. bleed) from electrolytic refinery
- Acidic effluent from the Precious Metal Plant
- Equalized effluent from ion exchange resin regeneration
The above effluents have a combined flowrate of approximately 80 - 100 m31h (22 - 27 Us),
almost 50 - 60 m3/h (14 - 17 Uis) originates from the smelter gas cooling and cleaning equipment
immediately upstream of the acid plants. The"weak acid' thus produced has a pH in the range
0.5-1.0 and a sulphuric acid concentration in the range 2.5% - 4.5%, with an averaae of
approximately 3.0%, a TSS of approximately 250 mg/L , cadmium 0.8 mg/L, copper 55 mg/L,
lead 33 ppm and zinc of approximately 7 ppm.
As shown in - Figure 4.2-2, the acid plant effluents are mixed with the acidic effluents from the
Electrolytic Copper Refinery, the Precious Metal Refinery and spent regenerant from the ion
exchange columns to make up a total of approximately 80 - 100 m3/h (i.e. 22 - 27 fs) of mixed
acidic effluent having approximately the following composition which was determined during the
recent site survey:
pH: 0.8; TSS: 170 mg/L; H2SO4: 2.5%; arsenic: 43 mg/L, copper 18 mg/L; iron 24 mg/L; lead 5.3
mgfL; manganese 0.3 mg/L; selenium 5.3 mg/L and zinc 1.8 mg/L.
Although the acid concentrations are realistic, the above analyses of the mixed"weak Acid"
show relatively low concentrations of arsenic and copper, reflecting possibly unsteady process
operation during the brief site survey conducted on July 23 - 26, 1997. A more extensive survey
of the effluents from the MDK plant should therefore be conducted in the future.  Any
observations and conclusions drawn in this report will, however, reflect the long-term and
historical characteristics of the water Which has been documented in the referenced reports.
This "weak acid" effluent is currently processed in a neutralization treatment plant followed by
settling of solids in the "Slime Lagoon". The clarified liquid is allowed to overflow into the
Pirdopska River, while the solids gradually fill the lagoon.
Based on the recent data, the clarified water discharged from the"Slime Lagoon" had a flowrate
of 23 Ips (i.e. 83 m3/hr), a pH of approximately 11.8 and an electrical conductivity of 3900S/cm
(see Appendix B - Water Analyses). The treated process effluent also had a total dissolved
solids (TDS) of 2800 mg/L, a total suspended solids (TSS) of 55 mgIL, fluoride 8.0 mg/L, a
sulfate concentration of 1600 mg/L, an arsenic concentration of 0.03 mg/L, copper 0.12 mg/L,
lead 0.078 mg/L, manganese < 0.005 mg/L, cadmium 0.003 mg/L, total chromium 0.006 mg/L,
molybdenum 0.45 mg/L, nickel < 0.01, selenium 0.85 mg/L, silver <0.01 mg/L, and zinc 0.021
mg/L.

Based on the above analyses, the treated process effluent was out of compliance with the
emission standards of category Ilil with respect to pH, electrical conductivity, TDS, sulphate and
selenium.
In past measurements, arsenic concentrations in the range 1 - 5 mg/L have been observed,
which are typical of the quality obtained from the current treatment method. As explained
above, the lower arsenic concentration obtained in the effluent during the recent site survey may
be due to unsteady process conditions upstream of the effluent treatment plant.
Detailed analyses of all process-effluent streams before and after neutralization are provided in
Appendix B for reference.
4.2.2.2 Treated Sanitary Wastewater
Sanitary wastewater produced at MDK is treated biologically in a two step process. Once
treated, the water is mixed with treated process waste streams and ultimately ends up flowing
into the Pirdopska River.  Alternatively, if the continuous pH monitoring indicates acidic
conditions in the treated sanitary waste stream, it is diverted to the lime neutralization plant
along with other acidic process streams. Although in relatively good repair, this wastewater
treatment plant (WWVTP) is of insufficient capacity for current loads.
In the MDK site survey conducted during the period July 23 - 26, 1997, the following
measurements were made:
- Flowrate: 9 I/s (32,4 m3/h)
- Quality: Treated effluent met all concentration limits for Ordinance No. 7, Category IlIl and
Category 11, except for selenium (0.35 mg/L vs. 0.01 mg/L under Category II and l1l)
4.2.2.3 Other Production Effluents
In addition to the above process effluent, process non-contact cooling water (i.e. from cooling
towers) is directed to the Sanar Dere Channel which flows into the Zlatishka River. Flowrates of
these streams measured during the recent survey (July 23 - 26, 1997) were reported earlier in
Table 4.2.-1.
4.2.2.4 Surface Run-off From 'Old" Eastem Part of the Plant
Rain water and drainage from the eastern 50 hectares flows into the Kiselo Dere stream at an
average flow rate of approximately 40 I/s. In the recent MDK site environmental survey, a flow
rate of 84 Us was determined in the Kiselo Dere. The Kiselo Dere flows into the Pirdopska
River. The concentrations of copper and arsenic frequently exceed the standard for the Kiselo
Dere (Category 11 Surface Water). These streams also contain a substantial concentration of
suspended solids. Sanctions have been charged against MDK for continuously exceeding the
environmental standards established by the government.

During the recent site survey, the concentrations of most parameters were found to be in
compliance with Category IlIl concentration limits, except for TSS (77 mg/L vs. 50 mg/L for
Category II and 100 mg/L for Category ill), copper (2.2 mg/L vs. 0.5 mg/L for Category ll) and
iron (21 mg/L vs. 5 mg/L).
4.2.2.5 Slag Flotation
To enhance the overall metals recovery from the smelter, flash furnace and converter slags are
treated in a slag flotation recovery system. The slag is treated by milling and flotation to
concentrate valuable metals, thus increasing recovery. The tailings of the process are pumped
as a slurry into a retention basin where water is recovered and recycled. According to a recent
SNC study, the current rate of deposition of total tails is 350 000 tpa. It is estimated that over
the 15 year life of the concentrator 4-5 million tonnes of tails have been deposited, with a total
volume of 3-4 million cubic meters. The allocated area for the basins has almost reached its full
capacity.
4.2.3 Treatment Facilities and Techniques for Monitoring and Control
4.2.3.1 Existing Acidic Effluent Treatment Process
In the existing "process effluent" treatment plant at MDK, the equalized acidic effluent is
neutralized with approximately 15-20 m3/hr (4.2 I/s) of lime containing approximately 5-15%
calcium hydroxide. The neutralization takes place in a 3-chamber interconnected in-ground
neutralization pool, with agitation provided by as many as seven air spargers in each of the
three chambers. The pH is controlled at approximately 12 using a single pH control unit located
in the second neutralization reaction chamber.
The overall dimensions of the neutralisation train are: 24 m x 4 m x 3 m, giving a liquid retention
time > 1.5 hours. Although this retention time is adequate, mixing and pH control are not done
efficiently, resulting in potentially excessive chemical usage and relatively poor water quality.
The mixed neutralized slurry has a flowrate of approximately 100 m3/h and contains mainly
calcium arsenite, calcium arsenate, copper arsenate, iron hydroxide, zinc hydroxide and other
metal compounds. The following specific concentrations were obtained from actual analysis of
the influent and the neutralized slurry obtained during the recent environmental assessment
conducted on July 23 - 26, 1997:
Equalised "Weak Acid" Effluent from the Plant Before Treatment:
pH: 0.8; electrical conductivity: 95000 pS/cm, TSS: 170 mg/L; sulphate: 25000 mg/L; fluoride 1.7
mg/L; arsenic 43 mgJL; cadmium 0.16 mg/L; calcium 22 mg/L; chromium <0.2 mglL; copper 18
mg/L; iron 24 mg/L; lead 5.3 mg/L; manganese 0.29 mg/L; nickel <0.50 mg/L, selenium 5.3
mg/L, titanium <0.5 mglL and zinc 1.8 mg/L.
