HATCH                                                                         Page 61
Neutralised Process Effluent
pH: 10.1; electrical conductivity: 7600 iLS/cm, TSS: 3.4%; sulphate: 1300 mg/L; fluoride 4.7
mg/L; arsenic 28 mg/L, antimony 0.13 mg/L, cadmium 0.16 mg/L; calcium 7700 mg/L; chromium
0.21 mg/L; copper 25 mg/L; iron 38 mg/L; lead 4.3 mg/L; manganese 1.5 mg/L; nickel 0.74
mg/L, selenium 3.5 mg/L, titanium 0.95 mg/L and zinc 1.4 mg/L.
This effluent overflows directly into a large lagoon (i.e. The Blue Lagoon) where the solids settle
and the supernatant overflows into a small creek which eventually pours into the Pirdopska
river. The 'Blue Lagoon" is almost full with inorganic sediment accumulated during the past few
years.
In addition to poor design of the neutralization plant, the following problems were identified:
-  The effluent is supersaturated with solids including calcium sulphate (i.e. gypsum) which
deposit along the path of effluent flow to the river.
- The pH of the effluent (i.e. 12) is too high for discharge. A pH in the range 6-9 would be
acceptable based on Bulgarian and Osparcom legislation.
Because of the high pH and intimate exposure of the neutralized effluent to the ambient air,
absorption of CO2 takes place. The reaction of excess lime and calcium arsenite/arsenate
solids with carbon dioxide produces calcium carbonate (i.e. limestone) and soluble
arsenous/arsenic acid, respectively. While the former provides an unsightly appearance
(bluish/greenish deposits of calcium carbonate caused by presence of copper and possibly
nickel compounds), the latter results in the introduction of "hazardous" soluble arsenic into
the receiving waters.
Amphoteric heavy metals such as zinc will be solubilized at the high pH of 12.
- Because of inefficient clarification, suspended solids are carried over into the outflow canal
or creek resulting in further deposition of hazardous compounds including calcium
carbonate, calcium sulphate, calcium and copper arsenate, etc.
4.2.3.2 Techniques for Monitoring and Control
The pH of the neutralized effluent from the neutralization plant is automatically controlled and
monitored continuously by MDK personnel. Since this is a single pH control system, its
accuracy is limited. However, the large 'alkali' Slime Lagoon provides a buffer in case of
malfunction in the pH control system.
While incidents relating to the acid plant mal-function in the past resulted in direct discharge of
strong acid into the river system, containment and automatic diversion of the acid flow to
standby tanks will prevent occurrence of such events in the future.
MDK has a reasonably well-equipped central laboratory which is capable of conducting most of
the chemical analyses reported earlier, including:
PR90579 Rev  . 91                                                     PRDOOT, Rev. 3, 9615
une,n%9Q579kdo4xUx9O579 doc



,IPL                                                     Union Miniere - EIA of MDK Smelter

pH, TDS, TSS, acidity, metals using ICP and various anions by titration and colourimetric
methods.
MDK conducts the following analyses on a regular basis:
- Copper, arsenic and sulphuric acid on acidic effluents twice per week.
- Copper, arsenic, iron, nickel, sulphate and dissolved solids in the treated water entering
Pirdopska River, daily.
- pH, copper and arsenic in site drainage entering Sanar Dere and Kiselo Dere, daily.
- Arsenic in the Topolnitza River at the village of Petrich, daily.
In addition, the Regional Environmental Inspectorate monitors the Topolnita River and its
tributaries at various points on a monthly basis. While Regulation 7 specifies that water
sampling to be conducted in the receiving water, after the water flowrate has stabilized (i.e. at
the Topolnitza River), the Inspectorate actually conducts sampling on the three water channels
from the MDK plant (i.e. Main Process Effluent Channel, Sanar Dere Channel and Kiselo Dere
Channel). Although the Sanar Dere and Kiselo Dere are classified as, Category II surface
waters and the Slime Pond channel are classified as Category IlIl, all penalties imposed with
respect to violation on pH, conductivity, sulphate, copper, arsenic, manganese, etc. are
calculated using the less stringent Category IlIl criteria. In any case, it is recommended that the
sampling points for the purpose of computing penalties be moved downstream close to the
points where the Zlatishka River and the Pirdopska River meet and pour into the Topolnitza
River.
4.2.4 Characteristics of Treatment Plant Sludge
The insoluble solids formed as a result of neutralization of the acidic effluents deposit in the
Slime Lagoon compact over a period of time, resulting in a "thickened sludge or cake". The
density of the solid varies with depth. The settled sludge consists approximately of the following
main components (100% solids basis):
Ca: 20%; As: 10%; S04: 50 - 60 %; oxygen, Mg, Si, Fe, Cu, Zn: balance.
MDK does not carry out any further treatment of the sludge. The Slime Lagoon is nearly full of
solids which have settled and accumulated over a long period of time.
4.2.5 Assessment of the Impact of Water Pollution on the Environment and Human
Health
The quantity and quality of water leaving the MDK site has a significant impact on the water
quality in the Topolnitza river and its tributaries in the Pirdop-Zlatitsa region. Indirect factors
PR90579 Rev 0 W8                                                      PR.DOT. Re. 3, 9605
p kun.vmok90579%docsWWr90579 doc



ZJ_L'' FUnion Miniere - EIA of MDK Smelter
HATCH                                                                       Page 63
affecting the water quality of the river system include pollution from the atmosphere via rainfall
and run-off from contaminated soils. The concentrations of dissolved substances, sulphate,
arsenic and copper have exceeded the maximum acceptable values over a long period, often by
a considerable amount.
Over the past two decades the environment surrounding MDK has suffered the impact of
increased industrialization and anthropogenation, although the former has played a far more
significant role. In 1970 the average concentration of dissolved substances in the Topolnitsa
River at Poibrene was 150 - 300 mglL, the pH was between 6.5 and 8.0, the sulphate
concentration was 30 - 70 mglL, the total hardness 6 - 10 mg eq/L, and the oxigen concentration
was on average between 3 and 6 mg 021L.
Between 1970 and 1987 MDK increased production levels while the surrounding area became
more densely populated. Waste treatment technology did not advance significantly during this
period and consequently, the level of pollution increased. At Poibrene, the concentration of
dissolved substances increased to 300 - 500 mg/L, the pH reached 8.5, while the hardness rose
to 10 - 16 mgeqlL. Toxic substances, such as arsenic and copper, had already exceeded the
maximum acceptable concentration (MAC).
Beyond 1987 new facilities were added to MDK and the first stage of its reconstruction and
modernization were realized. This added a greater stress on the local environment, as did the
load of increased urbanization. The Zlatishka river now registers a concentration of dissolved
substances at 1500 mg/L, a pH between 10 and 11, and a sulphate concentration of 100 - 200
mg/L. Arsenic and copper concentrations registered at 5 to 10 times their MAC values.
A study to determine the sources df pollution in the Topolnitza river and the impact on water
quality has been in progress since 1989. This involved regular sampling of the Topolnitza at
several points and also its tributaries in the Pirdop-Zlatitsa region.
These sample points are identified in Figure 4.2-3 and described as follows:
1.-  Topolnitza prior to the inlet of the Pirdopska
2.-  Topolnitza after the inlet of the Pirdopska
3. -  Zlatishka prior to the inlet of the Pirdopska
4. -  Pirdopska prior to the inlet of the Ziatishka
5. -  Pirdopska prior to its inlet into the Topolnitza.

The results of sampling at these positions with respect to heavy metals and minerals content are
detailed in the PHARE project report (Reference No. 2, Section 0.4). A brief summary of these
results is displayed in Table 4.2.5.
A direct correlation of water quality in the Topoinitza with influx of polluted water from the MDK
site via the Pirdopska river is not possible because of indirect influences. The results indicate
that the Topolnitza is contaminated with minerals and heavy metals (copper, zinc, cobalt and
manganese) prior to the junction with the Pirdopska and hence before the influence of MDK.
This is primarily due to discharge from the Medet mine tailings pond.
However, the Pirdopska river downstream of the discharge points from MDK shows the
increased presence of copper, manganese, iron and arsenic and has a high alkaliinity (pH 11).
These along with the Medet tailings pond discharge contribute to the total pollution load in the
Topolnitza. MDK is a major source of arsenic found in the Pirdopska, Zlatishka and Topolnitza
rivers.
Studies in the late 1980's showed arsenic at elevated levels, (in excess of 100 mg/litre). The
Topolnitza river at the village of Petrich had an arsenic content of 7 mg/litre. The waters from
the Topolnitza are an important source of irrigation for agricultural land along its length and for
the Plovdiv plain. As a result of the high levels of arsenic in the waters, treatment of high
arsenic Chelopech ore was prohibited at MDK in 1989. Although the water quality, in terms of
arsenic, has improved since then it is understood that arsenic in sediments in the Topolnitza and
its reservoir is still unacceptably high. Use of the reservoir water for irrigation is stopped when
the volume falls below a critical level.
The effect of arsenic and other heavy metals on health is discussed in Section 4.4.
4.2.6 Groundwater
The effect of solid wastes on the groundwater is discussed in Section 4.6B to provide an
opportunity to first discuss solid wastes and soils in Sections 4.3 and 4.6A.

4.3 Solid Waste
4.3.1 Production of solid wastes

MDK:

The fayalite tailings (tailings) and calcium-arsenite slimes (slimes) are stored in earth
containments located east and south of the plant as shown on Drawing 90579-002.
The solid wastes, their production and properties are discussed in Section 4.3.3.
At the present time MDK has not obtained a permit(s) for treatment of hazardous wastes and the
deposition sites have not been authorized in accordance with legislation (see Section 6.1.3).
4.3.2 Historic Operation
Since the start of MDK operation in 1958, the plant has produced large quantities of wastes,
has undergone many technological changes and has been subjected to similar changes in
environmental regulations. As a result the present plant site contains large quantities of solid
wastes that is located in piles, within abandoned buildings, buried and contained within earth
embankments.
Solid wastes at the MDK plant have been evaluated by three organizations:
1. Rudmetallurg Project JSCo. carried out an extensive inventory study to characterize,
qualitatively and quantitatively, the solid wastes on the MDK plant site that was reported on
October 1996 (Reference 5)
2. PHARE Project - Section 14 - Solid Wastes in the Region of Pirdop-MDK, Analyses of their
Type, Quantity, Quality and Accumulation Effects, April 1997. ( Reference 2)
3. UM/Hatch Technical Support Document 3, Environmental Impact Assessment of Solid
Wastes and Groundwater Conditions, August 1997.

The seven major categories of solid wastes are discussed in the following sections:
4.3.3.1 Process Categories of Solid Wastes
Slag was deposited between 1958 and 1988 in a large mound of 11 ha containing 6.3 million
tonnes of material. The slag contains high values of arsenic, copper and zinc.
The material is a copper smelting discard slag that consists primarily of fayalite (iron silicate).
The iron and silicon oxides constitute about 87 to 88% of the total weight. Small quantities of
base and alkali materials provide some neutralizing effect but the lime and magnesia are likely
wholly or mostly combined as silicate or not otherwise readily available because they are
dispersed internally in large chunks of the cooled slag.
The average results of chemical analyses of three samples of the slag are given in Table 4.3-3.

The levels of sulphur and copper are relatively high for discard slag. Some copper would be
present as oxides, but a large part as copper sulphide. Some of the sulphur would be combined
with iron and the other base metals present.
The dissolution is likely to be slow because of the coarse nature of the slag and the usually slow
kinetics of acid formation. However, because some toxic elements are present, it is necessary
to minimize the exposure of the slag to water and oxygen.
To confirm that the slag is not creating a serious risk on ground water contamination, leachate
testing according to EPA standards was conducted on the three samples and compared to
Bulgarian Ministry Guidelines (Reference 16). The range and average results of significant
parameters of the three samples are summarized on Table 4.3-4.

Fayalite tailings were deposited between 1988 and 1996 within a dyked area north of the
existing flotation plant. It is estimated that this area contains about 1.9 million tonnes of fayalite
tailings that contain low quantities of arsenic, copper and zinc.
In the initial years of operation all the tailings were stored in the large tailings pond just north of
the flotation plant. However, once it neared its capacity, a second small pond was constructed
for the purpose of dewatering the tailings slurry and sending the tailings offsite. In 1996, about
80,000 tonnes of the tailings were sold to the cement industry and the remainder was trucked to
the tailings pond at the Elatzite Mine, approximately 12 km from MDK.
The fayalite tailings are the discard fraction from the flotation plant which recovers quantities of
copper and other metals from the slag as a sulphide concentrate. The process involves
crushing of the fractured slag and removing the additional copper and heavy metals by means of
flotation using heavy medium. The resulting waste, fayalite tailings are pumped in a slurry form
into tailings ponds where the solids settle. The excess water is pumped back into the flotation
plant.
The tailings are a finely ground material consisting of about 10 percent of sand sized particles
and the remaining coarse to fine silt. Particle sized distribution of the tailings is given in Table
4.3-5.

It is assumed that the fayalite tailings have potentially less toxicity than the slag since their origin
is a slag which has undergone further treatment.
Calcium-arsenite slimes (slimes) have been produced since 1958 and deposited in a slime
pond south of the plant and in small lagoons located just south of the electrolysis plant. The
main storage area was the slime pond. However, when the slime pond reached several times
the design capacity, the small lagoons were used as a temporary storage until the slime pond
capacity was increased by raising the perimeter dam crest. Because of the small capacity of the
small lagoons, the slimes were excavated to provide additional storage capacity and the slimes
were deposited at several locations, with the main locations being:
- buried east of the small lagoons,
- concrete wall enclosure west of the electrolysis plant and
- north edge of the gIime pond

HATCH                                                                       Page 73
The ore that was smelted previously from the Chelopetch Mine contains relatively high contents
of arsenic and gold. Some of this gold has been precipitated into the slime. There has been an
interest in the past to recover the gold from the slimes and possibly use the profits from the gold
to pay for the reclaimation of the existing slime pond. Aside of the environmental problems that
would be posed in the gold recovery process, there had not been an accurate evaluation on the
concentration of the gold in the siime pond until this year, documented in the work by
Rudmetallurg and the PHARE Project.
The PHARE Project (Reference 2) reported gold concentration in the range of 5 to 15 g/t and an
average of 7 g/t. The work recently completed by Rudmetallurg showed that the concentration
of gold varied with depth from 0.3 gft near the surface to 6.6 g/t at maximum depth of 12 m.
Considering that the largest volume of slimes are near the surface because of the geometry of
the pond depression, it is reasonable to assume that the average gold concentration is about 2
g/t. At this concentration is not economical to extract the gold and pay for the slime pond
reclaimation.
Converter furnace dust (CFD) is the only covered waste storage area at the plant.
Toxicologically the most hazardous waste with high contents of toxic metals including (As, Cd,
Cu, Pb, Se, Sb, Zn) and high sulphate and acidity. Human exposure (inhalation, skin contact) is
made possible by the fine dust particle characteristics of the waste, and represents an extreme
potential hazard. This material is recovered from the converter fumace electrostatic precipitator
and contains significant amounts of copper which ideally should be recovered by recycling back
to the smelting process. However, it also contains substantial amounts of impurities which may
be detrimental to the finished copper product.   Many copper smelters operate a
hydrometallurgical process to remove these impurities and recover the copper in a form suitable
for recycling.

to lesser extent in the east part of MDK site. High levels of Cu, sulphates, As and Pb are

Other solid wastes that are found in smaller quantities on the plant are:
Linings and deposits for the roasting plant, gas pipelines and stack. The toxicity and potential
hazards rank it second to the CFD. High levels of toxic metals (As, Cd, Cu, Pb, Se, Sb) and
sulphates together with high acidity (- 1,5 pH in leachate) are the main characteristics of this
material. Its potential risk is enhanced by the fine-grained nature of waste (dust potential).
Process additives and media. Relatively heterogeneous material consisting of; deteriorated lime
(still useable for neutralization when needed), Rasching rings (As, Cu, Pb, sulphates), spent
vanadium pentoxide catalyst from the contact acid plant (V, C, sulphates, acid reaction).

pipes and within decommissioned transformers and hydraulic gears.
Plant debris and soils contaminated by metals and/or oil, spread all over the entire plant area.

Insulation materials consisting of- glass fibres, cement-asbestos. These are located mainly at
the north side of the old metallurgy and sulphuric acid production building. Preliminary
separation prior to disposal is necessary. The material has been impacted by Cu, As and
sulphate.  The metal toxicological effects are accompanied by the carcinogenic effects of
asbestos in its dust particles.
Steel scrap and process equipment. This material can be managed using standard recycling
techniques, although the cleaning of the material and linings may still pose a heaith and safety
concem.
4.3.3.3 Abandoned Buildings.
A large portion of the southern part of the MDK is covered with abandoned, and some collapsed
buildings that will have to be demolished and removed as early as possible. While these
buildings take up valuable space, the main detriment of these buildings is that precipitation
soaking these structures produces contaminated water, both surface and groundwater. Drawing
90579-101 shows the general location of the abandoned buildings.
Some.of the major abandoned buildings and structures are:
> old smelter complex
- old acid plant
old training centre
unfinished water treatment plant
old heavy fuel storage area containing surface and buried tanks

PHARE Project Conclusions
The above study concluded the following:
1) Prevent soil and air contamination by dust produced by wind erosion of the old fayalite
tailings pond.
2) The slime pond has limited capacity to store additional calcium-arsenite. Should investigate
improved technology for treating effluents from acid plant.
3) Present methods of storing electrolysis tubs do not confirm to hazardous waste storage
guidelines. Provide facility for storing/disposing this product to meet the hazardous waste
storage requirements.
4) Spent vanadium catalyst shall be stored in a designed facility.
5) Wastes from production buildings shall be sorted, assessed and classified in categories.
Process/dispose of these wastes safely.
PR90579 Rev 0, 9508                                                 PR.OT. Rev. 3. 96/05
p \>nm,n%90579 doc'V%p9Q579.doc



Ji2                                                      Union Miniere - EIA of MDK Smelter

6) Most of the landfills and storage areas for solid waste have not been licensed and do not
meet requirements for safe storage and therefore are sources for releasing and
accumulating of wastes in the region of the plant.
7) A more detailed assessment of dangerous water and soil contamination with toxic soluble
components of solid wastes requires leachate testing.
4.3.4 Assessment of Solid Waste Handling and Disposal Facilities
The handling and storage of solid wastes at MDK does not comply with Bulgarian and
International regulations nor with common international copper smelter practices. UM/Hatch are
in general agreement with the PHARE Report conclusions.
The following are highlights of our assessments:
I1) Fayalite Tailings The main risk associated with the tailings is due to the very fine particles
that have no cohesion. As a result, this material has a great potential for wind erosion when
dry with the dust settling on surrounding land. The settled dust is not a desirable material
because it contains elevated arsenic and heavy metals that are not acceptable for
agricultural and residential areas. As discussed in Section 4.1, dust from this source
frequently contributes to the higher than permissible levels of heavy metals in the air in
Pirdop and Zlatitsa.
2) Slime Pond The existing slime pond is filled to capacity and the perimeter dam has
marginally stability. The slime has only about 0.5 m freeboard left (distance between the
pond water level and dam crest) which is marginal for a pond containing hazardous slimes.
The pond has experienced past seepages through the dam section and these are again
occurring. The seepages are an unsafe phenomenon because the seepage areas can
enlarge with time and the thus lowers the stability of the dam. Finally, the slimes are in a
near liquid state. Any instability of the dam would result in the majority of the enclosed
slimes to run into the Topolnitsa River.
3) Solid Waste Piles The material in many of the waste piles scattered around the MDK plant
are hazardous.  Precipitation falling on these piles causes contaminated water to
contaminate adjacent streams, the underlying soil and the groundwater.  Soil and
groundwater contaminations are discussed in section 4.6.
4) Old Fuel Storage Area The old fuel storage area contains large puddles of oil and diesel fuel
that have flowed out of broken steel and concrete storage tanks.  Most of the tanks,
including underground tanks, have remnant fuel and the soil in the whole area is
contaminated from fuel spills. Presently the contamination of soil and groundwater by
petroleum is limited. However, with time this will increase.

HATCH                                                                      Page 77
5) Abandoned Buildings A large portion of the southern part of the plant is covered with
abandoned buildings, with several of them in a collapsed state. The building materials and
remnant equipment is contaminated by dust and the buildings contain remnants of copper,
lead, etc. by-products. Precipitation penetrating the collapsed buildings causes hazardous
wastes to contaminate surface waters, underlying soils and groundwater.
4.3.4.1 Assessemnet of the Impact on Environment and Human Health
The large quantities of contaminated and some hazardous solid wastes are producing an impact
on the environment and human health as follows:
- Storing contaminated solid wastes on natural ground causes the underlying ground to
become ever more deeply contaminated
- Precipitation falling on the contaminated solid wastes and abandoned buildings causes the
contaminants to be spread widely through surface water and groundwater
-Wind born dust from the uncovered portion of the fayalite tailings pond and from other
contaminated areas is polluting the atmosphere and the land and buildings in neighbouring
towns and agricultural areas.
Each of the above is a potential risk to the health of the population and the ecosystem.
4.4 Toxic and Other Dangerous Substances
4.4.1 Toxic Substances
The characteristics and chemical composition of raw materials, products and waste materials
from the production activities together with various waste materials found within and around
MDK have been described in the previous sections. These indicate the presence of a variety of
toxic substances
The toxic effects of the relevant substances on human health and the ecosystem are described
below:
Vanadium and Vanadium pentoxide
Human Health Effects
Inhalation
Workplace exposure to vanadium and vanadium compounds could lead to irritation of the
respiratory tract, nausea and pigmentation of the mucous membranes.
Skin and eye
Contact dermatitis and conjunctivitis may occur after exposure to Vanadium dust.

HATCH                                                                      Page 78
Carcinogenicity
There is no evidence that Vanadium poses a risk to carcinogenic, mutagenic or teratogenic
effects.
The main daily maximum admissible norm for the general population amounts to 0.002 mglm3.
Ecotoxicological Effects
Vanadium and Vanadium pentoxide have moderate ecotoxicological effects on the aquatic
environment. Appropriate and relevant toxicity data for the terrestrial environment are generally
lacking.
Cadmium
Human Health Effects
Inhalation
LC50 inhalation, rabbit: 45 mgIm3/4 h (Cd dust) - reference: Fielder & Dale, 1983. Toxicity Rev.
7. Health & Safety Executive, HMSO, London. Inhalation of Cd in workplace conditions may
lead to severe irritation of the airways followed by dyspnoea, cough and cyanosis. Symptoms of
metal smelters fever may occur, such as: cough, headache, dizziness, weakness, chills, fever,
chest pains and shortness of breath. The symptoms can be preceeded by a latent period. In
severe cases fatal chemical pneumonia have been described after extreme high exposure
conditions.
Ingestion
LD50 oral, rat : 2330 mg/kg (Ref. Fielder & Dale, 1983. Toxicity Rev. 7. Health & Safety
Executive, HMSO, London). Ingestion results in nausea, vomiting, headache and abdominal
cramps.
The main exposure source of Cd for the general population is through the food chain in
particular the consumption of vegetables. Smoking doubles the average intake.
The main daily maximum admissible norm for the general population is 0.00002 mg/iM3.
Skin
Cadmium fumes can be irritating to mucous membranes.
Eyes
Cadmium fumes are irritating for the eyes.

Chronic Toxicity
Prolonged environmental or workplace exposure may cause effects on the kidneys resulting in
proteinuria and kidney dysfunction. Obstructive airways disease and pulmonary fibrosis have
only been described in workers exposed to Cd dust or fume for prolonged periods. Some
deaths due to emphysema have been reported in workers repeatedly exposed to very high
levels of Cd fume.
Carcinogenicity
Cadmium metal has not been classified by the EU, as a carcinogen.
Cadmiumoxide has been classified by the EU, as a carcinogen category 2 (may cause cancer
by inhalation).
Irmitation/Sensitisation
Cd dust can be irritating for eyes, nose and throat.
Ecotoxicological Effects
The soluble Cd ion is considered as ecotoxic for the environment; especially algae are sensitive
to bio-available Cd exposure.
The transformation rate of insoluble Cd forms (incl. Cd metal) towards soluble ions, is however
rather slow.
Lead
Human Health Effects
Inhalation/lngestion
LC50 inhalation and LD50 oral data on rats, for Pb metal powder are not available.
Over-exposure in workplace conditions through inhalation and/or ingestion of Pb containing
dusts or fume may result in loss of appetite, anaemia, malaise, insomnia, headache, irritability,
muscle and joint pains, muscle weakness, gastritis and liver changes. Heavy exposure to
certain specific lead compounds such as Pb acetate and Pb phosphate has been associated
with decreased fertility and increased abortion of pregnant women.
Skin
No irritation or skin sensitisation has been reported.
Eyes
No eye irritation has been reported.

Chronic Toxicity
Prolonged workplace or environmental inhalation or ingestion of high concentrations of lead dust
(or fume) may cause impaired neurotransmission and immune system function and may
increase systolic blood pressure. Kidney damage has only be reported by workers in these
circumstances.
Irrftation/Sensitisation
No skin sensitisation or irritation has been reported.
The main exposure sources of Pb for the general population is food. However, uptake of dust
via pica behaviour could contribute up to 50% of the Pb uptake of small children. The main daily
and mean annual maximum admissible norm for the general population is 0.001 mg/M3.
Ecotoxicological Effects
There is a general lack of appropriate and relevant toxicity data on Pb. Studies on Pb salts
however show that the soluble Pb ion could cause moderate ecotoxic effects to organisms of
different Taxonomic and Throfic levels.
The transformation rate of insoluble Pb forms (incl. Pb metal) is very slow.
Copper
Human Health Effects
Inhalation
Inhalation of Cu oxide fumes in workplace conditions may cause metal fume fever characterised
by chills, fever, aching muscles, dryness and metal taste, headaches, sneezing, nausea and
irritation of nose and trachea.
Ingestion
Oral TDLo human: 0.120 mg/kg.
Ingestion of Cu containing dusts under extreme high workplace conditions may irritate the
intestinal tract.
Skin
Copper fumes can be irritating to skin and mucous membranes.
Eyes
Copper fumes are irritating for the eyes.

Chronic Toxicity
Prolonged exposure in workplace conditions may lead to hemolysis, absence of urination,
hypertension and convulsions, kidney and liver damage.
Irnitation/Sensitisation
Possibility of: - eye irritation
- irritation of skin and mucous membranes
- irritation of the intestinal tract if swallowed.
The main daily maximum admissible norm for the general population is 0.01 mg/M3.
Ecotoxicological Effects
The soluble Cu ion has shown to be ecotoxic in the aquatic ecosystem, in particular-for fish and
algae. The terrestrial toxicity of bio-available copper is moderate. Vertebrates are generally not
very sensitive towards Cu exposure with the specific exception of sheep, which are highly
sensitive for Cu through oral intake.
The transformation rate of insoluble Cu forms (incl. massive Cu) to bio-available Cu is very slow.
Arsenic
Human Health Effects
Inhalation
Dust or fume inhalation under workplace conditions may cause severe irritation of the lungs,
upper respiratory tract or a perforated nasal septum. Systemic arsenic poisoning may occur
with symptoms of weight loss, nausea, diarrhoea, weakness, loss of appetite and skin lesions.
Ingestion
LD50 oral rat: 763 mg/kg. Arsenic is harmful by ingestion.
Severe irritation of lining of stomach and intestines may occur. Ingestion of arsenic may cause
also arsenic poisoning and gastrointestinal disorders.
Skin
Dust or fume may cause severe irritation, dermatitis or contact dermatitis.
Eyes
Severe irritation of the eyes or conjunctivitis may occur.

Chronic Toxicity
Damage to liver, kidneys and nervous system occurred in the past after severe workplace
exposure to As.
Carcinogenicity
Arsenic has been ciassified by IARC as group 1 carcinogen. Group 1: the agent is carcinogenic
to humans.
IrTitation/Sensitisation
Severe irritation of eyes and skin may occur.
The main daily maximum admissible norm for the general population is 0.003 mg/m3.
Ecotoxicological Effects
Arsenic has moderate ecotoxic effects on the aquatic ecosystem. The transformation rate of
insoluble As forms to bio-available As is, however, rather quick. As in water and sediments can
be removed from these compartments through methylation by bacteria.
4.4.2 Assessment of Influence of Pollution by Dangerous and Toxic Substances on the
Environment and Human Health
The interpretation of the above effects and environmental fate characteristics indicate that
improper storage of dangerous substances at the MDK-AD plant could potentially lead to
exposure of these dangerous substances to the environment and the human population.
However, proper control, packaging and storage conditions could overcome further exposure to
the environment.
4.5 Harmful Physical Factors
Physical factors contributing to the overall environmental pollution of an industrial facility include
noise, vibration, and radiation. At MDK, the only physical factor with the potential to contribute
to the environmental pollution generated from the smelter is noise.
4,5.1 Noise
Noise is generated on a continuous basis by process and related auxiliary equipment (e.g., fans
and pumps), and on an intermittent basis by vehicle traffic transporting material in and around
the facility at MDK.
Typical of other copper smelters, noise generated from the operation of process equipment at
MDK is not excessive and is further attenuated by building structures. Process equipment is not
a source of environmental pollution at MDK. Auxiliary equipment located outside of production
PR9Of79 Rev. O 9S3                                                 PRMOOT. Rev. 3. 96.05
p- unionmtn%90579Nc 90579 doc



Union Miniere - EIA of MDK( Smelter
HATCH                                                                         Page 83
buildings are a potential source of noise pollution, and for MDK, this is limited to fans and pumps
used to transport process gases and liquids.
The fans and pumps currently in use at MDK are designed for low pressure, high volume flows,
and as a result do not generate excessive noise levels. This is validated by noise level
measurements along the border of the smelter as reported in the Phare Report. The measured
noise levels did not exceed 60 dBA, and the maximum admissible noise levels for industrial
areas is 70 dBA (Sate Gazette No. 87/1972 and No. 16/1975).
Noise generated from vehicle traffic in an around the MDK smelter also has the potential to
contribute to the overall environmental pollution generated by the smelter.  However, the
intermittent nature of this noise source minimises its overall impact as is demonstrated by the
measured data. The localised, short term effect of noise generated by truck traffic can be
minimised by proper maintenance of truck exhaust systems and routing that avoids populated
areas.
4.5.2 Radioactive Contamination
There is no known radioactive contamination at the MDK smelter, as verified by UM.
4.6 Soils
4.6.1 Areas Within the Facility
The MDK plant site is heavily contaminated from nearly 40 years of operation that started in
1958. The contamination is the result of operation of the smelter, acid and electrolysis plants,
deposition of hazardous wastes within the plant and various stage of collapse of abandoned
structures. An overview of the contaminated areas is shown on Drawing 90579-002.
In an overall visual assessment of the plant, 15 areas were identified to be potentially
contaminated. The level of contamination for these areas was investigated by means of
sampling solid waste materials on the surface, surface soil sampling, test pits and drill holes.
Representative samples of wastes, suspected contaminated soils and water were submitted for
chemical analyses. The following areas were investigated in detail:
- abandoned heavy fuel area
- abandoned smelter area
- abandoned sulphuric acid area
- small lagoons
electrolysis plant
> hydrochloric acid tanks
copper sulphate plant
> miscellaneous solid waste piles.
PR90579 Res. 0. 95/O5                                                 PROOT. Rev. 3. 96105
p: untonrmtn90579%docsvor90579.doc



Jil                                                     Union Miniere - EIA of MDK Smelter
HATCH                                                                       Page 84
The contamination different areas is illustrated on selected photographs given in Appendix A
and on Drawings 90579-002 and 90579-005 in Appendix B.
The environmental assessment was performed in a staged approach as given below.
Visual Assessment
The plant was visually inspected to determined potentially contaminated areas which would
require a more detailed evaluation. This assessment was made based on the current
operations and the management of toxic materials. The assessment focused on the old part
of the plant, especially in areas around abandoned buildings, as these were the most likely
areas to be heavily impacted.
- Waste Piles
During the visual assessment of the site, 15 piles of solid materials were found. Samples of
these wastes were collected for chemical analyses to determined their level of
contamination, if any. Management options for these materials could then be assessed
based on the level of impact. The analysis undertaken included pH, selected metals and
anions and/or total petroleum hydrocarbons (petroleum), as appropriate.
- Surface Soil
In areas with evidence of surface soil contamination, soil samples were collected and
chemically analysed for metals and hydrocarbons.
- Test Pits
In heavily contaminated areas, test pits were excavated to depth of 3 m.  The soil
stratigraphy and contamination were visually examined and samples were taken at 1 m
intervals for chemical analyses. A total of 10 test pits were excavated.
- Drill Holes
Twenty-two drill holes were advanced on the plant site to determine the soil stratigraphy,
depth of contamination, and groundwater level. In each drill hole, soil samples were
selected for chemical analyses. Water samples were also collected to determine water
quality. In 6 drill holes, pumping tests were performed to determined the hydraulic
conductivity of the overburden material.
Twenty-one of the drill holes were advanced to depths ranging from 6 to 20.5 m. The final
depth advanced was decided in the field based on the level of contamination encountered in
each drill hole. One drill hole was advanced to 30 m to locate the bedrock surface as
bedrock was not encountered in the first 21 drill holes at the plant site. None of the drill
holes at the plant site encountered bedrock, including the 30 m deep hole.

4.6.1.1 Chemical Analyses
Soil, water, and waste samples were sent to a laboratory for chemical analyses. Two suites of
chemical analyses were selected for the soil and waste samples analysed:
determination of moisture content (reported as %solids), As, Cd, Cu, Mn, Ni, Pb, V, Zn, and
pH
- determination moisture content and petroleum hydrocarbons concentrations.
Samples were also analysed to determine the Sb, Se, chlorides and nitrates concentration, if
necessary.
The drilling and laboratory chemical testing was managed ESS Engineering Service Sofia Ltd.
The chemical testing for the drill hole samples and water samples from piezometers was
performed by Laboratory Geological Investigations Ltd., Sofia. The laboratory is authorised
under:
Geotechnical Investigations    No. 83 - N/30.07.1996
Petroleum and Coal Geology No. 82 - N/30.07.1996
The protocols from drilling and the results from the chemical testing of soil and groundwater
testing are given in Technical Support Document 7.
Chemical testing of solid wastes, which also had been conducted by Rudmetall Project in
October 1996 (Reference 5), surface and test pit samples was conducted by Hatch. These
results are summarised in this report and given in detail in Technical Support Document 3.
The results from chemical tests of soil samples are discussed under major plant areas below.
4.6.1.2 Chemical Quality Criteria for Soil Contamination and Groundwater
The results of the analytical testing were compared to Bulgarian, Belgium and International
criteria and standards. The following tables summarise the key criteria reviewed.

For the assessment of the soil quality, the Belgium criteria were preferred over the Bulgarian
criteria. Bulgarian criteria are based on agricultural land use and do not provide criteria for the
assessment and remediation of an industrial site. Belgium criteria are risk based criteria for the
remediation and clean-up of industrial properties, and are similar in magnitude to soil criteria for
industrial sites elsewhere in the world and were determined based on the protection of human
health for the site and surrounding areas. Criteria for the Netherlands and Canadian clean-up
criteria are also provided for comparison.

HATCH                                                                         Page 87
Bulgaria does not currently have groundwater criteria applicable for the assessment or
remediation of a site. Surface water criteria do exist and are applied to the surface water
receptors of the runoff (Category II Criterion) and effluent discharges (Category III Criterion)
from the site. These criteria are not discharge quality but rather receptor quality criteria.
The groundwater assessment was completed by comparing the groundwater analytical results
to the Belgium  groundwater criteria.  These criteria are designed for the protection of
groundwater resources and potential human use. As indicated on Table 4.6.1.2-2, the Belgium
groundwater criteria are generally more stringent than both the Bulgaria Category II and
Category IlIl surface water criteria.
4.6.1.3 Soil Contamination Assessment
4.6.1.3.1 Soil Overburden
The 22 drill holes advanced to a maximum depth of 30 m did not encounter bedrock. The soils
were found to be irregularly stratified clays, clayey silts to silts, sandy clays and sand layers with
varying of gravel and boulder content [to 70%]. The heterogeneity of the stratigraphy is the
result of the alluvial origin of the material.
Generally the soil has a high silt and clay content at the surface and becomes coarser with
depth. At about 5 to 7 m the soils become sandy and gravelly and contain a small percentage of
fines. The coarse material below 6 m depth contain silt and clay layers.
Bedrock was encountered at a depth of 11 m below ground surface at the slime lagoon, about
600 m south west of the plant. The presence of bedrock at these depths indicates that the plant
is located over an area where the bedrock is depressed.
4.6.1.3.2 Abandoned Fuel Area
A large abandoned fuel storage area covering about 25,000 m2 is located in the central westem
portion of the plant. The abandoned fuel area contains a number of abandoned oil storage
tanks and is heavily impacted with spilt fuel oil. There are several pools of oil and the tanks
contain residual fuel oil. The depth and volume of these residuals are unknown. The major fuel
tanks and contaminated areas are shown on Drawing 90579-003 and are listed below:
- 4 steel above ground tanks: two with 400 m3 and two with 1,000 m3 capacities
- 4 concrete round tanks, partially buried, each with 350 m3 capacity,
- Concrete rectangular tank with total estimated capacity of 3,000 m3,
- remnants of an old fuel storage area
remnants of two railroad sidings, and
- 2 above ground tanks with a 45 m3 capacity south of the main storage area.

HATCH                                                                       Page 88
The above steel and concrete tanks are in various stages of demolition. Two of the steel tanks
are partially collapsed and the round tanks were decommissioned by punching holes into the
sides.
Fuelling areas are visibly contaminated by oil leakage which occurred during the handling of
fuel.
The extend of the fuel contamination was determined by visual inspection and using a
hydrocarbon vapour analyser. The purpose of the analyser was to determine the extent of
underground fuel contamination.
In summary, this area contains abandoned fuel tanks that have not been completely drained.
Many have been ruptured resulting in ponded fuel beside the tanks. Results from the soil gas
probing and soil and groundwater testing show the following:
all surface samples having visible fuel contamination had petroleum hydrocarbon contents
above criteria
hydrocarbon vapour probing show the area of fuel contamination to be extensive.
two tests pits dug to a depth of 3 m beside the heavily contaminated areas due to fuel
transfer activities show the contamination to be limited to less than 1 m depth based on the
analytical results from samples collected at depths of 1, 2 and 3 m
> in areas within the tank containments where ponded fuel is present, the depth of
contamination is anticipated to be deeper.
drill holes show the contamination to be limited to the near surface soils
4.6.1.3.3 Abandoned Smelter Complex
An abandoned smelter complex is located in the south central part of the plant; just north of the
administration building. This complex consists of several buildings with a total size of about 60
by 300 m. The complex is in great disrepair and contains partially filled fumaces, scrap metals,
concentrate and other materials.
The area was evaluated by the following testing:
- hydrocarbon vapour probing
- composite surface soils and waste piles samples at locations L-3,4,5,24-26 and W-37-48.
- a test pit in the south-eastern part of the complex, and
- drill hole is located on the south side of the building, down gradient of the building to
evaluate contamination at depth and groundwater quality as shown on Drawing 90579-103.
In summary, two contamination areas were identified outside and close to the south wall of the
old smelter building.

1. A solid waste pile located at of the building consists of soil mixed with neutralisation sludge.

2. A heavy fuel impacted area, about 200 m2 in size, is located by south-westem corner of the

was evaluated by petroleum vapour probing and soil samples, showed hydrocarbon levels
above criteria.
Inside the building, where furnaces and casting systems are located, there are mixed wastes of
debris, linings and equipment deposits. They all have Cu and sulphate concentrations in the
order of 104 - 105 mg/kg and As, Pb and Sb (102 - 103 mg/kg). The floor sweepings have a
petroleum hydrocarbon concentration of about 5000 mg/kg.
The inside of the northern section of the smelter building are partially decommissioned blowers,
filters and pipelines for transferring the roasting gas to the old sulphuric acid plant. Various
waste piles were sampled and analysed. Among them were masonry and deposits of the main
structure, solid waste piles, linings and gas pipe deposits and insulation material, glass fibres
and cement asbestos). The linings and pipeline deposits showed high acidity of 1,4 pH and had
the highest contamination levels for As, Cd, Pb, Sb, Zn, and sulphates. Similar, yet lower
concentrations occurred in other samples. The soil around the dry filters showed high levels of
As, Cu, Pb, and sulphates.
Drill hole shows the highest concentration of Cu at 11.2 m.
4.6.1.3.4 Abandoned Sulphuric Acid Area
The abandoned sulphuric acid plant covers and area of about 5 ha. It consists of several
structures and the overall area contains three solid wastes piles. The structures within this area
mostly collapsed and are heavy contaminated with acid.
Three solid waste deposits within this area are:
Deposit I is located in the middle of old sulphuric acid production plant - the wastes mainly
contain old stack demolition materials of about 600 tonnes of fly dust, 10 tonnes of scrap and
700 tonnes of debris and contaminated linings.
Deposit II is also located in the middle of old sulphuric acid production plant - consisting of about
200 tonnes of scrap and 50 tonnes of debris.

follows:
- Soil along the railroad siding dividing the old acid plant and old smelter (L-27 through 30) is
heavily contaminated by metals; with maximum values being: As (9 000), Cu (20 000), Pb
(30 000), Sb (1 200), sulphates (100 000) mg/kg and hydrocarbons up to 3 000 mg/kg. The
soil pH ranges from 4.7 to 2.8.
- Lower levels of contamination in the soil and debris were found at the northern boundary of
the smelter. The prevailing pollutants are petroleum hydrocarbons (up to 4 500 mg/kg) and
suiphates (up to 100 000 mg/kg). The metals concentrations are below the criteria values.

sulphates up to 10 000mg/kg.

up to about 3 000 mg/kg, probably from the contact reactor fillings.
> Other waste types found in this area are linings of decommissioned equipment, demolished
stack, roasting gas tubes and absorption towers filling (Rasching rings. High As, Cd, Cu, Pb,
Sb and Se was found in the gas tubes. Rasching rings contain high Cu, Pb, As, and Sb at
levels of hundreds of mglkg. The pH of all samples ranged between 1,7 and 1,1.
- The most potentially hazardous waste is converter dust in a pile. The toxic elements Cd,
Cu, Pb, Se, Sb, and Zn range from hundreds to hundreds of thousands of mg/kg.
- Samples from Drill Hole 12 show elevated concentrations of Cu, Pb and As in samples at 2
m and 5 m depths.

The small lagoon area is located in the south-west comer of the plant and consists of three
lagoons, about 40 m by 90 m each, buried residue waste to the east of the lagoons, and a

4.6.1.3.6 Electrolysis Plant
The electrolysis plant, located in the western section of the facility, is the only plant assessed in
this report that was actively used by MDK. The plant consists of a two story building, about 50
m by 180 m in size. The electrolysis tubs (766 pcs) are located on reinforced concrete structures
which form an elevated deck. The floors are made of acid-resistant floor tiles inclined towards a
central drain pit. The tiles do not cover the area containing plant rail trackage used to transport
copper anodes from the smelter plant.
PR90579 R*v. 0. 951W                                                  PROOT. Rev. 3.96'06
p un,m90S79or9579 doc



2~L                                                      Union Miniere - EIA of MDK Smelter
HATCH                                                                         Page 92
The electrolysis operation produces large seepage of electrolytes and salts. These seepage's
have weakened the concrete structure and have heavily corroded exposed steel. The floors are
to a large extent covered with copper substrate deposits. The electrolyte fluids have penetrated
the underlying soils with the greatest penetration occurring in the transportation area.
Used tubs, about 400 units, are stored west of the electrolysis building. It is estimated that the
tubs represent about 720 tonnes of solid waste material.
The contamination produced by the electrolysis plant has been investigated by means of surface
soils samples and hydrogeological drill holes.
In summary, surface (foundation floor) soil sampled in the south and north side areas of the
building indicate a massive surface contamination of petroleum, As, Cu , Pb and Sb. As the
floor is mostly acid - proof tiled, deeper contamination depends on the floor integrity.
Soil sample taken at the effluent outlet of the electrolyses plant, where spent electrolyte was
being discharged, showed some sulphate and elevated As.
Solid samples from the tubs show the tubs to be high contaminated with As, Cu, Ni, Pb, Sb and
sulphates. Rainfall has rinsed these tubs and contaminated the underlying soils with metals (As,
Cu, Pb, Sb, Cd, Mn, Ni, V and Zn.)
4.6.1.3.7 Abandoned Hydrochloric Acid Tanks
Four tanks with a capacity of 25 mr each, containing hydrochloric acid, are located north of the
precious metal electrolysis plant. These tanks used to provide hydrochloric acid for the
production of precious metais. However, leakage from the tanks indicates that some contents
remain.
The tanks are constructed from steel plate and are lined inside with PVC material. The tanks
are elevated above the ground resting on concrete columns. The ground surface below the
tanks is lined with acid-resistant masonry.
The steel tanks are heavily corroded, in some places as deep as up to the inner. In one
instance, small acid drips were observed.  The entire ground surface exhibits strong
contamination with hydrochloric acid spills and aggregates of salt.
In summary, the masonry base below the tanks has neutral pH and is not significantly impacted.
Samples of solid waste from below the tanks and a surface soil sample show contamination by
acid leakage and seepage into the ground. Both samples are acidic with a pH range of 2, 1-2, 6

4.6.1.3.8 Abandoned Copper Sulphate Production Plant
This plant is located directly east of the precious metal electrolysis plant and north of the main
electrolysis plant (1.2.19). This plant is not being used.
The inside of the building is contaminated with copper sulphate deposits on both the plant
machinery and building components. The building contains abandoned machinery, storage
tanks, fumaces, electric motors etc..
In summary, solid wastes and leachate from equipment located in the southem and northem
parts of the building have high Cu, Pb, Cd, and sulphates concentrations, as well as elevated As
and Sb concentrations.
The masonry and mortar plaster on the Northeast side of the building close to the heavy fuel
piping is contaminated with oil and copper sulphate. Flooring is contaminated by deposits
contain copper sulphate and other metals (As, Cu, Pb and Sb). The leachate is highly acid.

Used Vanadium Pentoxide-Contact Catalyzer 11.4

and underground storage tanks, pipes and within decommissioned transformers and hydraulic
gears.
Transformers - No PCB contamination was found in the samples tested.
Plant debris and soils contaminated by metals and/or oil, spread all over the entire plant area.
The oil contamination traces mainly the former fuel oil systems.

4.6.1.4.3 Building Demolition
Various structures are to be demolished as part of the site development. The majority of the
structures to be demolished are reinforced concrete skeletons with brickwork curtain walls.
There are also assembled skeletons, classical brickwork buildings with wooden roof trusses and
steel structures of varying composition. The structures to be demolished are in poor and in
some cases even hazardous condition.
To demolish low story brickwork .and skeleton structures with brickwork curtains, mechanised
demolition methods can be used. The same methods can also be used for high story skeletons,
although blasting technology could be tested for this purpose.  Steel structures must be
demolished using stepwise dismantling and cutting.
Prior to starting demolition activities, all waste and processing equipment in the area must be
removed and power supply disconnected.
4.6.1.4.4 Debris Composition
The resulting debris for all structures except steel structures is expected to have the following
composition:
- 75% building structure debris
- 27% waste

4.6.2 Groundwater
The soil overburden in the plant changes with depth from being clayey soils near the surface
and becoming coarser with depth. The clayey silt to silty clay surface soils provide a partial cap
for the groundwater regime with horizontal groundwater flows confined to the deeper sand and
gravels.
The groundwater surface was observed to be generally about 7 to 8 m below the surface with
the flow being to the south to Southwest towards Topolnitsa River. The hydraulic gradient is
between 0.02 and 0.04. Based on pumping test results of six deep wells, the hydraulic
conductivity of the overburden materials was calculated to range from 5x10 ' up to 6x10-5 m/s.
The groundwater quality at the MDK plant was investigated by the installation of 22 piezometers
at the plant and 10 piezometers in outlying areas of interest, namely, 4 piezometers in the slime
pond, 4 piezometers in the tailings and 2 piezometers in the slag dump for a total of 32
piezometers. Three of the piezometers were installed above the plant, tailings and slag area to
determine the background water quality from above of the plant. One piezometer east of the
slime lagoon and two piezometers south of the lagoon provide some indications of the water
quality below of the plant.
The groundwater quaiity was evaluated against Belgian groundwater criteria applicable to
industrial sites as explained in Section 4.6.3.
4.6.2.1 Plant Area
The impacted groundwater is wide spread across the site with the groundwater at most locations
exceeding the groundwater criteria used for the assessment (Drawing 90579-005). The drawing
outlines the contaminated water areas by isopachs indicating the factor by which the water
quality exceeds the Belgian Water Quality (BWQ) criteria as follows:
- water exceeds BWQ
- water exceeds BWQ by a factor of 10
- water exceeds BWQ by a factor of 100
Seventeen of the 22 piezometers installed within the plant area containing the main production
buildings exceeded the Belgian groundwater quality criteria.  Seventeen of the groundwater
samples exceeded at least one criteria value by more than a factor of 10, while six samples
PR90579 Rov. 0, 95103                                               PR.DOT. Roy. 3. 9M5
P-unimmM90579\4Pr90579 aoc



Union Miniere - EIA of MDK Smelter
HATCH                                                                         Page 99
exceeded at least one criteria by more than a factor of 100. Finally, four of these samples
exceeded the copper criteria by a factor of more than 1 000.
The most heavily impacted groundwater, those which exceed a criteria by more than a factor of
100, are located in the Southwest corner of the site near the abandoned smelter area,
electrolysis workshop, small lagoons and hydrochloric acid storage tanks.
Results of analytical testing from well nests south of the electrolysis plant show that the
groundwater in the deeper, more permeable materials have higher contaminant concentrations
than the shallow groundwater in less permeable soils. This is likely due to dispersion and
transport effects in the higher permeability materials which allows contaminants to spread over
larger areas.
Groundwater samples collected on the upgradient (northern) and eastern portions of the site
have low levels of metals with no or only marginal (<10 times guideline value) exceedances of
criteria values..
Groundwater samples from monitoring wells located along the down gradient edge of the site
have low metal concentrations but do indicate marginal exceedances of criteria values for
copper and cadmium.
While the level of groundwater impact on the site is high, the mass transport of contaminants off-
site is relatively small. It is estimated that the horizontal flow velocity through the soils with
permeability is about 25 m/year.
This rate of migration is considered to be appropriate but conservative given that the copper
contamination identified in the area of the small lagoons and the old smelter area have not yet
been detected at BH-8, approximately 400 metres down gradient. The fact of very slow
migration of the contaminants, possible dilution of the contamination with distance and
absorption of the contamination by the fine grained soils, it is hard to estimate, if or when the
contamination may reach Topolnitsa River.  However, even if the contamination reaches
Topolnitsa River, the slow flow/release of the contaminant into the river, may be difficult to
detect.
However, considering the high groundwater contamination at the plant site, further studies of the
contamination flow and potential effect on Topolnitsa River will have to be evaluated in greater
detail.
The piezometers installed downslope of the slime pond (4), fayalite tailings (4) and slag dump
(2) show that the groundwater is not contaminated according to the Belgian Groundwater
Criteria. Only one element in the 10 water samples had one component, cadmium, elevated
above these criteria.

4.6.3 Adjacent Areas
The main sources of industrial pollution in the Pirdop - Zlatitsa valley are mines, ore dressing
plants and metallurgical activities.  They produce liquid and aerosol wastes containing
substances which have the potential to damage the soil. In respect of this, many investigations
have Deen made on the pollution of the agricultural lands, crops and livestock in the Pirdop-
Zlatitsa valley.  A major study was commissioned in January 1997 by the Ministry of
Environment and funded by the PHARE programme, to assess the impact of emissions from
MDK on soils in the Pirdop Zlatitsa region. An interstitial report of the project which is being
undertaken by the Balkan Science and Education Centre of Ecology and Environment was
issued recently. This is a comprehensive study and is a valuable reference for information
relevant to this section.
The following provides a brief summary of information gathered in this and previous studies, and
in particular the Report on the Environmental Impact Assessment of MDK in Operation by
Pechlivanov Engineering. These studies are also used as the reference source for Section 4.7
Flaura and Fauna.
4.6.3.1 Charactenstics of Soil in the Region
Fluvisols
These are represented by deliuvial and alluvial soils. The delluvial soils occupy the northem
and central parts of the valley and are characterised by a thin layer of humus-accumulation,
under which are delluvial materials. The soil resistance to atmospheric contamination depends
to a high degree on their mechanical composition. Soils with a heavier mechanical composition
are more resistant and have higher absorption properties. Among the other factors which
influence the behaviour of heavy metals in the soil, their mobility and passage into other media,
are the pH and humus contents of the soils. The reactivity of these soils varies from very acid to
slightly -acid and even to neutral. Humus contents are low (1-2 percent) which is a detrimental
factor from the point of view of atmospheric contamination influence. Organic matter contents in
the soil are considerably more efficient than mineral components as an absorber.
The hydro-physical properties of the delluvial soils are characterised by low moisture-retaining
properties and low reserves of useful moisture.
Alluvial soils can be found on the flood plains of the Topinitza, Bunovska, Mirkovska, Pirdopska
and other rivers. Alluvial layers, consisting of gravel, sands, sandy clays, etc., form the humus
accumulating layer of not more then 30 to 40 cm. Humus content in these soils reaches 4 to 5
percent. The pH of the soils is acid to neutral, turning alkaline at depth with pH 8.3.
Luvisols
Luvisols (cinnamon forest soils) have been found over an area of approximately 10 000 decares
on the higher regions of the valley. Their humus layer is thin as a result of the existing erosion
PR90579 Rw. 0. 95108                                                  PR.DOT, Rev. 3.96105
p:\U,orm,,90579%doeslr4w90579 doc



Union Miniere - EIA of MDK Smelter
HATCH                                                                          Page 101
processes and the humus contents are iow (1 to 2 percent). Soil pH varies in depth from acid or
neutral on the surface layers to slightly alkaline at depth where there are carbonates present.
Dystric-Eutric Cambisols
Brown forest soils can be found at altitudes over 800 metres on the mountain slopes. The low
absorption capacity of these soils, their light mechanical composition and their acid reactivity
lead to their low resistance to atmospheric contamination. They are characterised by an
exceptionally thin profile, with a humus depth of 4-5 cm. Humus contents and total nitrogen
contents are comparatively high.
4.6.3.2 Rising Soil Acidity
This occurs as a result of the industrial emissions to the atmosphere; in particular sulphur
oxides, carbon dioxide, chlorine compounds.  The acidity has risen exceptionally in the
contaminated soils, reaching levels at which exchangeable acidity appears, causing plant
toxicity. The control delluvial soils do not show the existence of such acidity levels. The process
of rising acidity is accompanied by the destruction of the clayey structures of montmorilonite and
illite types, while acid degradation of the soils at an alkaline saturation of approximately 10
percent being directed towards the formation of clayey structure of the oxide-hydroxide types.
These last types have an exceptionally low absorption capacity and reduces the buffer
properties of the soil.
The acid emissions do not only effect directly the acidity of the soil, but cause other effects as
well by changing the soil reactivity. First of all, this is the high mobility of the heavy metals in the
soil and the rising quantity of the exchangeable and absorbable forms. Up to 57 percent of the
total copper contents of the soil are in an exchangeable absorbed state, while in non-
contaminated soils these forms of copper contents do not exceed 1-2 percent. The acidity of the
soil causes the release of copper ions as well as other heavy metals. Due to the high mobility of
most elements in acid soils, this causes the soil to lose important macro-elements required for
the normal growth of vegetation. Due to the high mobility of the metals in an acid environment,
the risk of these metals passing into and contaminating other environments (vegetation, ground
waters, lower soil horizons, water reservoirs, etc.) rises sharply.
4.6.3.3 Heavy Metal Contents of the Soils
Copper is the main heavy metal contaminant of both agricultural soils and forest soils. In
Bulgaria the highest accumulation of copper in soils has been found in the Zlatitiza valley with a
value of 2 724 mg/kg. Contamination by other elements,including zinc, lead, arsenic, has also
been established.
In soils where acidity has increased, there is an increase in water soluble metal forms which are
the forms which affect plants the most. The water soluble forms of copper exceed 50 percent,
those of lead exceed 18 percent, those of zinc exceed 12 percent. According to data from the
Ministry of Agriculture, the following maximum values of heavy metal contents have been found:

- copper - 2574 mg/kg (101-fold above the maximum admissible value)
- lead - 628 mg/kg (23-fold above the maximum admissible value)
The total area of soil which is considered to be contaminated by heavy metals and the degree of
contamination is as follows:
- Dangerously contaminated, 54 626 decares, 5-fold in excess of maximum admissible
value(MAV)
- Very strongly contaminated, 45 924 decares, 3 to 5 times MAV

Slightly contaminated areas, 478 decares, higher than normal but below the maximum
admissible value
The above data does not include soils of the forest areas, since precise calculations of their
contaminated areas have not been made. In forest soils the worst contaminant is also copper.
In the 1 to 5 cm soil layer the copper contents are 3 to 4 times higher than those of the
underlying soil. This high degree of accumulation in very acid soil provides conditions for high
mobility of the copper and its transfer to lower strata of the profiles. Lead and zinc are also
contaminants of the soils. The emissions of these heavy metals over decades have led to their
accumulation in the dead forest carpet where values of up to 7 600 mg/kg of copper, 716 mg/kg
of lead and 169 mg/kg of zinc have been found.

On the base of these investigations and surveys it can be established that:
- the total Cu, As, Pb, Zn and Cd content in the surface soil layers from the surveyed part of
the region exceeds not only the mean background values for the country but also the
permissible levels for these elements
= the content of some of the elements in the crops exceeds the permissible levels
the spatial variation of the pH and total forms of Cu, As, Pb, Zn and Cd content in the surface
soil horizons depends not only from MDK atmospheric emissions but from many other
sources
> the water soluble sulphates content in the soils in this region is close to the background
values
From the same investigations and surveys it was assumed that:
- the main route for the crop contamination is atmospheric pollution
- the soil acidification, increase in Cu and As concentration in the soil surface layers and
increase in Pb, Cu, As and Cd concentration in the crops can be connected to MDK
- the increase in Cd, Pb and Zn concentration in the soil surface horizons can be connected
with the proximity of Bimak-AD
- there are some similarities between Pb and Zn, Cu and As soil contamination, and between
Cu and As crop contamination.

4.7 Flora and Fauna
4,7.1 Flora
The forest in the area of MDK is European deciduous forest consisting of oak, hornbeam and
beech according to the height of the land. In the immediate vicinity of MOK, vegetation is mainly
beech and oak plantation and man made conifer plantations. These forests have been strongly
affected for decades by air pollution and other types of anthropogenic activities. A part of the
affected plantation on the Southern ridges of the Balkan Range have been repiaced with more
resistant deciduous species such as acacia, silver linden, red oak. A number of studies have
been undertaken to establish the affected areas and the effects of sulphur dioxide, sulphuric-
acid aerosols and heavy metals aerosols. These studies took into consideration the average
annual concentrations of SO2 adopted by the Economic Commission for Europe, for the tree
species of differing sensitivity: 50 g/m3 - highly sensitive; 80 g/m3 - sensitive; 120 g/m3 - low
sensitivity.
The results of the previous investigations have shown that the impact of gas emissions on the
plants begins with the penetration of polluting substances into the leaves and the supervening
impact on cell processes, after which changes take place in the assimilation organs, due to the
intensive gas-exchange, absorb polluting substances. The observation of various tree species
in the Pirdop area using 19 indices for structural and functional digression shows that the
possibilities for adaptation of the studied tree species have been reduced. Test plants situated
at a considerable distance from the industrial works are affected as well as plants in the vicinity
indicating that that the impact of industrial emissions extends over more distant areas as well as
in the immediate vicinity.
The chemical analyses of leaf samples and common pitch pine needles from investigated test
areas at various distances from MDK (5, 7.5 and 20 km in the direction of Klissura) have shown
that the acidification of the soil and the deposition of heavy metals in it as well as their direct
deposition on the leaves lead to the accumulation of considerable quantities in the foliage. The
greatest accumulation is of copper in the pine needles which is three to eight-fold above the
average values for the area (5 - 8 mg/kg) at 5 and at 7.5 km from from MDK. Concentrations of
copper above the average but considerably lower are established at a distance of 20 km.
(Irrespective of the fact that copper is one of the biogenetic elements, the plants need it in small
quantities: 5 - 20 ppm.) In the investigated tree species at a distance of 7.5 km copper exceeds
20 ppm, reaching 40 - 60 mg/kg. Beside a high copper content, higher values of zinc and
manganese are also noted, the latter being above 2000 mg/kg, exceeding by two to three-fold
the average for the area. The concentration of zinc is up to 80 mg/kg and does not exceed the
toxic levels.
The data provides the basis for discussing the dependence of histological and adaptation
capabilities on the degree of soil pollution. Up to a certain degree of pollution, at 5 to 10 km
from the works, adaptation is related to zeromorphous changes of the epidermis, which correlate
PR90579 R. 0. 95108                                                   PR.DOT, R.~ 3. 9605
p UPmu1c99057?doc9%p,=r90579 doc



ZIK                                                       Union Miniere - EIA of MDK Smelter
HATCH                                                                        Page 105
to the considerable growth in the number of leaves. At high degrees of pollution between 2 to 4
km from the works the expansion of the cells in the covering tissue may be indicative of
destructive changes.
Of the indeces examined, current linear growth, number of leaves and their size, investigations
have shown that the most sensitive is the leaf size. In the vicinity of 0.5 to 1 km of MDK is
reduced to 65 percent of the control for the black poplar, to 70 percent for the hawthorn, 76
percent for the acacia, 82 percent for the birch and 90 percent for the spiraea. The same
tendency is established for the tree species in the Kaliman area - at 10 km, but the values are
close to the control. The depressive impact of sulphur dioxide on the growth and the sizes of
the leaves has been frequently noted.
The contents of individual elements in the plant organs depends to a great extent on the
species. The plants of some species have a higher susceptibility to absorbing individual
elements without receiving apparent damages. The quantities of heavy metals in plants are
determined both by their contents in the external environment, and by their selective ability. A
strict correlation between the contents of heavy metals in the plants and in the soil is not always
observed.
The contents of mineral elements in the investigated plants can be graded in an ascending order
in the following succession: Pb - Zn - Mg - K - Fe - Ca. In cases of heavier industrial pollution, a
greater accumulation of Fe and Cu is established from two to five-fold more than in the control.
Differences have been established in the degree of accumulation and is also determined by the
specifics of the plant species.
4.7.2 Fauna
The fauna species in the Pirdop-Zlatitza valley are defined by the predominance of the Mid-
European species. Studies for evaluation of the impact of contamination from MDK on the fauna
started in the 1970s.
Amphibeans and reptiles
Within an area of 7 km2 around MDK, especially to the east and north east, all the amphibian
and reptile species have been exterminated, possibly due to lack of nourishment in the
grassless and eroded terrain. Another important factor causing the extermintion of the
amphibian species in the area around MDK is the change in the characteristics of the local water
reservoirs. These are gradually filling with alluvial materials, becoming more and more shallow
until disappearing.
The established habitats of Rana dalmatina and Hyla arborea are not sufficient to determine the
areas in which these species have not been exterminated, but the data available indicates that
their habitats are within the same areas, as that of the Rana ribidunda. During the reproduction
period, they visit the large water reservoirs in the vicinity of the area contaminated by MDK. The
PR9S579 Rev. 0. 95=06                                                 PRDOT. Rev. 3. 96'05
p \un.onm,n\90579kaocx¢xS0579 doc



Union Miniere - EIA of MDK Smelter
HATCH                                                                      Page 106
large numbers of Rana ribidunda, Rana daimatina and similar species near the contaminated
areas to the south and south west of the Combine are due to the formation of swamps. Frogs
are rarely found in the contaminated waters
The populations of Bofo viridis and the rarer brown frog and Triturus cristatus are especially
affected by the contamination of the area. In addition to the lack of nourishment, there has been
a sharp drop in the moisture content of the lowest air layer in the grassless and eroded lands.
The reduction in the population of amphibian and reptile species in the area of the Combine is
the main reason for the disappearance of the yellow water snake.
Insects are represented by a very poor variety of species and no insect eating mammals were
found within 500 m of MDK. As the distance increased some moles were found. Rodents were
found at 3 km from MDK.
Birds
No connection between the direction of nest sites and the location of MDK could be established.
The proximity of MDK does not influence the species composition of the birds. The bird
population of the settlements in the MDK area has also been established to contain a variety of
species. The bird population of the agricultural cultivated lands to the east of Pirdop and to the
west of Zlatitza, as well as to the south of MDK, is typical for this landscape. In the immediate
vicinity of MDK certain species have been observed. The couples nesting nearest to MDK are
in the southern direction (within the affected zone) , where the surviving vegetation reaches the
Combine itself. The affected zone to the north and north east of the Combine is not inhabited.
Fish
Studies have been conducted to 'establish the accumulation of certain heavy metals and toxic
elements in fish. The values for the following heavy metals and metabolically active elements
were established: cadmium, lead, copper, cobalt, nickel, manganese, zinc, magnesium, iron and
strontium.
The lowest concentrations of the studied elements were established in the fish in the Dushatzi
dam reservoir and in the river Topolnitza upstream and downstream of the said reservoir. This
area can be classified as naturally clean and can be used as a basis for comparison with other
areas. The fish in the Topolnitza river upstream of the Jekov Vir dam reservoir and in the same
reservoir do not contain different quantities of accumulated toxic elements. After the inflow of the
Zlatishka and Medetska rivers, which are completely void of life, the situation in the Topolnitza
river becomes much worse. The river is completely void of fish up to the villages of Petritch,
Kayryaka and even up to the village of Poibrene. The fish in the Topolnitza river in the upstream
part of the Topolnitza dam reservoir have been found to contain 2 to 5 times higher
concentrations of toxic elements. The degree of accumulation varies very widely, but the toxic
metal concentrations in the fish species of the higher trophic levels are higher.
