Heavy Metal Contents and State of the Populations of the Biologic Indicator Amphibian and
Mammal Species of the MDK Area
Studies on the effect of the industrial contamination by MDK on the fauna in the area of the town
of Pirdop have been conducted mainly be the Institute of Zoology at the Bulgarian Academy of
Science. These studies have been conducted in three stages as described below.
The first stage covers 1973 to 1975. The main results were as follows:
- The negative influence of MDK's emissions was determined in correlation with the prevailing
wind directions on the composition and habitat of amphibian and reptile species in the area.
This influence was established to be specially strong on the population of Bufo viridis and
Triturus cristatus.
- No influence was established on the bird population.
- Studies of mammals have also been conducted, and prior to 1973 the existence of toxicosis
in sheep fed with grass from the Pirdop area was proved. In 1973, changes in certain
biochemical systems and hematological characteristics in white rats which were exposed for
one and three months in the MDK area were established. Higher pathologic changes were
established at the end of the first month in the number of erythrocytes, in haemoglobin and
from cases of reticulocytosis and sozinfilia. After the third month the rats adapted to
conditions.
Studies in 1977 on the population and habitat of rodents and insect eating mammals established
a direct influence of the source of contamination from 300 to 700 m.
The second stage of the investigation was completed in the'1980s. Of the vertebrae species
studied, the average results of heavy metal contents are quoted in the Table 4.7-1 below and
are compared with the measurements obtained in a clean area (Regional Station Steneto in the
Troyan mountain range.

5. ANALYSIS OF COMPLIANCE AND DEVIATIONS FROM LEGISLATIVE
REQUIREMENTS, RISK ASSESSMENT
5.1 Air
The characteristics of the emissions from the stacks at MDK are discussed in section 4.1.
Section 6.1.1 describes the limiting parameters for air emissions stipulated by Bulgarian
environmental legislation which the company should comply with in the course of the operation
of the smelter. The emission criteria required by other jurisdictions are also provided for
comparison.
In summary:
- emissions of sulphur dioxide from the acid plant stack (120 metre) exceed the maximum
acceptable emission (MAE) by a factor averaging about 400 percent
- emissions of sulphur trioxide are slightly in excess of the MAE by a factor of about 1.2
emissions from the 325 metre stack exceed the MAC for dust by a factor of between 8 and
12 in 1995 and 1996
-- emissions from the 325 metre stack exceed the MAC for sulphur dioxide by a factor
averaging 1.2, although in previous years when the limit was higher the emissions were well
below the standards
In general the emission criteria stipulated by the Bulgarian legislation are comparable to those in
other jurisdictions (e.g. EU-Osparcom BAT).
Although the emissions from the 325 metre stack exceed the limits, it is considered that they are
not sufficient to pose a serious risk- to the environment and the health of the local population in
view of the height of the release point into the atmosphere. Discharge of sulphur dioxide and
sulphur trioxide from the acid plant (120 m stack) have the potential to cause significant impact
on the local environment and population particularly during periods of abnormal operation or
climatic conditions which are unfavourable for efficient dispersal of the stack plume.
Of greater concem are the low level fugitive emissions from the smelter operations and from the
dry waste material storage areas.  These are difficult to quantify but ambient air quality
measurements in Pirdop and Zlatitsa show that concentrations of sulphur dioxide, sulphuric acid
aerosols and dust, frequently exceed the permissible limits by Bulgarian and comparable other
standards. Sulphur dioxide and acid aerosols are known to cause respiratory problems. Dust
from the smelter is likely to contain a range of heavy metals including copper, arsenic, cadmium
and lead, all of which have the potential for harm to human health.

5.2 Water
The MDK copper smelter discharges surface run-off water from various parts of the plant into
two small tributories, viz. the Zlatitsa River and the Pirdopska River. MDK surface water to the
Zlatitsa River comes from the Sanar Dere Channel while the Kiselo Dere Channel pours into the
Pirdopska River.
Process effluents are neutralized, clarified and released into the Pirdopska River after combining
with treated water from the Sanitary Treatment Plant. The Zlatitsa River joins with the Pirdopska
River and then pour into the Topoinitsa River a few kilometers south of the plant.
5.2.1 Discussion of Applicable Legislative Requirements
MDK's treated process effluent entering the Pirdopska River is required to meet Bulgarian
Ministry of Environment and Water's "Flowing Surface Water" quality criteria defined in
Regulation No. 7 of December 1986, Category No. III, as shown in Table 5.2.1. MDK will pay
penalties for any parameters which are out of compliance with Category No. III guidelines. The
penalties are based on the concentration of the parameters in excess of Category IlIl values, as
well as the flowrate of the effluent stream. An example of this calculation for excess arsenic is
given in Technical Support Document 2.
Surface run-off waters from the MDK smelter carried by the Sanar Dere channel and Kiselo
Dere Channel and pouring into the Zlatitsa and Pirdopska Rivers, respectively, must meet
Category No. II criteria, which is substantially more stringent than Category No. Ill. While
Ordinance 7 specifies that water sampling be conducted after the water flowrate has stabilized
(i.e. at the Topolnitza River), the MEW Regional Inspectorate actually conducts sampling on the
three water channels from the MDK plant (i.e. Main Process Effluent Channel, Sanar Dere
Channel and Kiselo Dere Channel). Although the Sanar Dere and Kiselo Dere are classified as
Category II surface waters and the Main Channel is classified as Category Ill, all penalties
imposed with respect to violation on pH, conductivity, sulphate, copper, arsenic, manganese,
etc. are calculated using the less stringent Category Ill criteria. In any case, It is recommended
that the sampling points for the purpose of computing penalties be moved downstream close to
the points where the Zlatishka River and the Pirdopska River meet and pour into the Topolnitza
River.
Guidelines of the Bulgarian MEWVs Regulation No. 7, Category No. Ill are compared in Table
5.2.1 with other countries' Maximum Allowable Concentrations of Pollutants in Effluents, such as
Canada, USA, Japan, Chile, Belgium (Flanders) and EU (current and Draft Osparcom).
PR9O579 Re  0. 951                                                    PR.DOT. Rev 3. 9b05
p %LMonm nk90579%dr90S79 dce



ZG~ Union Miniere - EIA of MDK Smelter
HATCH                                                                        Page 111
Comparison of the maximum allowable pollutant concentrations imposed in various countries
shows the following:
- The pH criteria range of 6-9 is the same for almost all of the countries compared.
- The total suspended solids criteria varies between 50 mg/L and 150 mg/L
- Amongst dissolved solids, the sulphate ion limit is set to 400 mg/L by Bulgarian MEW. This
is lower than the limits set for example in Belgium (2000 mg/L) and in Chile (1000 mg/L).
- The concentration limit for arsenic varies between 0.1 and 0.5, with most countries using a
value of 0.5 mg/L, including canada, US, Japan and Chile. It should be noted that current
Standard Industry Practice used in copper smelters around the world (i.e. with
neutraliazation, clarification and filtration) achieves an arsenic concentration in the treated
effluent in the range 1-5 mg/L.
-  The criteria for copper under Category III is 0.5 mg/L. This is typically achievable by
Standard Industry Practice.
- Cadmium limits vary between 0.01 and 0.3 mg/L, with Bulgarian Category IlIl set at a very
low value of 0.01 mg/L compared to Osparcom (0,08 mg Cd/I as yearly average).
- Lead limits from copper smelters are set by Osparcom at 0.5 mg/L as total lead. This
compares with 0.2 mglL under Bulgarian MEW's Category IlIl and any where between 0.2-
1.0 mg/L in Canada, Belgium, USA, Chile and Japan.
Mercury is controlled by many countries at low concentrations in the range 0.001 - 0.02
mg/L. Category IlIl limit is consistent with Osparcom : 0.02 mg/L.
-- Selenium is not regulated by many countries, although it is known to have significant aquatic
toxicity effects. Bulgarian MEW regulates selenium under Category IlIl at 0.01 mg/L which is
extremely low, and is equal to Canadian Drinking Water Quality Objectives.
5.2.2 Discussion of Compliance and Deviation from Legislative Requirements
Analytical parameters of actual MDK treated process effluent discharged to the Pirdopska River
are also listed in Table 5.2.1 for ease of comparison to regulatory concentration limits.
Comparison of actual MDK's treated effluent composition data from the Blue Lagoon (obtained
from recent on-site sampling during the period July 23 - 26, 1997) with Bulgarian Surface Water
Category IlIl criteria can also be made by reference to Table 5.2.1. This Table shows that most
of the heavy metal concentrations in the treated effluent are below the Category IlIl limits. The
following parameters are out of compliance:
pH (11.8 vs. 6-9); electrical conductivity (3900 vs. 1600 pLS/cm); fluoride (8 vs. 3 mg/L); sulphate
(1600 vs. 400 mg/L) and selenium (0.86 vs. 0.01 mg/L).
In summary, the concentration limit criteria imposed for neutralized process-effluents, based on
immission standards (concentration in the receiving water), are not relevant! The evaluation of
the MDK effluents should be conducted in comparison with emission standards as done in the
EU and other countries.

Regulation No 2 on the Maximum Admissable Hazardous Contaminant Levels in Communities
Ambient Air and in the Standard Levels of Permissable Emissions( Cocentrations of Waste
Gases) of Harmful Substances emitted into the Atmosphere.
The following articles of the latter regulation on emission standards are applicable to MDK:
Articles No 13 and 14 apply to emissions of dust-like substances.

Article 20 applies to the sectorial standards for the emissions from production processes of
heating capacity from 500kW to 50 MW. At MDK the combustion gases from processes which
burn oil are discharged via the 325 metre stack. The relevant limits are:

-  CO 170 mg/Nm3
- Dust: 50 or 80 mg/Nm3
-  S02: 1000 mglNm3

with respect to MDK emissions from the 325 metre stack:
- dust emissions are limited to 20 mg/Nm3
sulphur dioxide emissions are limited to 800 mg/Nm3
for an oxygen concentration of 12 % in the off gas
Article No 29 applies to the sectorial standards for the production of sulphuric acid and is
relevant to emissions from the 120 metre stack:
- the sulphur dioxide emission should not exceed 2.6 kg per tonne of 100% sulphuric acid
produced
-- sulphur trioxide emissions should not exceed 0.6 kg per tonne of sulphuric acid produced in
existing installations and 120 mg/Nm3 for new plants

authorities or jurisdictions, are given in Tables 6.1.1 and 6.1.2.

6.1.2 Water
6.1.2. 1 Introduction
The MDK copper smelter discharges surface run-off from various parts of the plant into two
small tributories, viz. the Zlatitsa River and the Pirdopska River. MDK surface water to the
Zlatitsa River comes from the Sanar Dere Channel while the Kiselo Dere Channel pours into the
Pirdopska River.
Process effluents are neutralized, clarified and released into the Pirdopska River after
combining with treated water from the Sanitary Treatment Plant. The Zlatitsa River joins with
the Pirdopska River and then pour into the Topolnitsa River a few kilometers south of the plant.
6.1.2.2 Bulgarian Standards
The Bulgarian environmental legislation has not yet developed general or sectorial standards for
the water quality of the emitted effluents.
Regulation No. 7 of December 1986 only gives indices and norms for assessing the quality of
the 'receiving surface waters" (i.e. immision values). These Receiving Water Standards are
summarized in Table 6.1.2.1 below:

Surface run-off from the MDK smelter carried by the Kiselo Dere and Sanar Dere channels and

6.1.3 Waste
Most of the wastes resulting from the production activities of the company, contain hazardous
substances which have the potential to pollute the environment. At present the company has
not obtained a permit for treatment of hazardous wastes and the depository sites in current use
have not been authorised in accordance with Decree No 153/1993 on the Gathering,
Transportation and Neutralisation of Hazardous Wastes.
With respect to the forthcoming construction of new depository sites as part of the
Environmental Remediation Programme after privatisation, the company must comply with the
provisions of the Environmental Protection Act and Decree No 153/1993 concerning treatment
of hazardous waste.
The authorisation of existing waste depository sites as well as construction of new ones has
three major aspects:
=- determination of the situation of the sites under the procedure set forth in Decree No
153/1993
- approval of the Environmental Impact Assessment of the site by the Minister of the
I Environment
- obtaining a permit for treatment of hazardous wastes
6.2 Short & Long Term Compliance And Remediation Programmes
6.2.1 Programmes for Bringing the Process into Compliance with Legislative
Requirements
6.2.1.1 AirEmissions
UM's first priority for MDK is to stabilise production operations while gradually increasing
production levels. The key to stabilising operations relies on being able to feed the flash furnace
with a uniform blend of high grade concentrate at a consistent feed rate. To achieve this goal,
UM will:
- increase the concentrate storage capacity to 50,000 tonnes, and
- refurbish and upgrade concentrate handing and blending equipment.
By improving the feed, the operation of the smelter will be brought under control, and this will
allow UM to optimise the operations of the furnaces, converters, gas cleaning facilities and acid
plants, and other related equipment.
PR90579 Rev 0. 95108                                                PR.DOT, Rev. 3. 96/05
D %unmomnXS90579d 79 doc



zxl                                                        Union Miniere - EIA of MDK Smelter
HATCH                                                                          Page 119
This will allow the operation of the waste heat boiler to be optimised (re-instate the gas recycle
loop) to limit the production of SO3 and prevent overcooling of the off-gas. Higher flow rates and
a reduction in the SO3 content of the off-gas stream will improve the cleaning efficiency of the
ESPs, which in turn has a positive impact on the acid plant efficiency.
Similarly the use of higher grade copper concentrate as feed to the smelter will produce a higher
grade of matte. The time required for blowing the matte in the converters will be reduced thus
allowing correct sequenceing of the converter, which will improve the sulphuric acid plants
efficiency.
Stabilising operations will have a significant, positive impact on reducing the air emissions
released from the smelter. By ensuring optimal operation of equipment, fugitive emissions
released will also be minimized.
In addition to stabilising operations, several specific investment programmes have been
identified to bring air emissions from the MDK smelter into compliance with legislative
requirements. In the following sections, the proposed programmes are described in general
terms followed by a planning strategy for reducing air emissions.
6.2.1.1.1 Fugitive Emissions from Fumace Operations
Fugitive emission sources from furnace operations were detailed in Section 4.1.2.3. As part of
UM's strategy to stabilise operations, fugitive emissions will be largely eliminated by redesigning
the gas flows, optimizing ductwork, fansytem and hooding and minimize leakage.
6.2.1.1.2 Drying Furnace Emission Control
The poor performance of the concentrate dryer ESPs is a principal factor in the high dust
concentrations currently being emitted to the atmosphere via the 325 m  stack.  Dust
concentrations in excess of 1200 mg/Nm3 have been measured at the outlet of the dryer ESPs.
To help reduce the dust emission levels to the legislative requirement of 20 mg/Nm3, the ESPs
have to be decommissioned and replaced with a fabric filter baghouses. This option is under.
study by UM. The current design flow rate two dryers operating is 144,000 Nm3/hr.
Emissions of sulphur dioxide from the concentrate dryer are due mainly to burning high sulphur
oil. However a significant amount may be generated by ignition of the concentrates due to poor
temperature control in the kiln. It is envisaged that there will be a reduction in sulphur dioxide
emissions as a result of:
- a more consistent throughput of uniformly blended concentrate allowing a more stable
operation and hence more precise temperature control

6.2.1.1.3 Process Steam Boilers
Exhaust gas from the waste heat boilers is currently exhausted to the atmosphere via the 325 m
stack.  The boilers may be decommissioned once smelter operations are stabilised and
sufficient quantities of steam are produced in the waste heat boilers to satisfy process
requirements. This will eliminate this pollution source.
6.2.1.1.4 Sulphuric Acid Plant Emissions
Stabilising operations will have a significant impact on reducing emissions from the acid plant by
providing a constant tenor, high volume SO2 gas stream to the acid plants. This will allow the
plant to operate within its design limits.
To ensure emission targets are achieved, improvements in the acid plant will include:
- equipment repairs and upgrades
- implementation of a regular maintenance programme
- improvements in flow and operator control with ductwork and fan system upgrades
Two double contact acid plants are a prerequisite to comply with the legal requirements. In this
respect, UM will investigate the feasibility of converting the single contact acid plant into a
double contact acid plant.
6.2.1.1.5 Material Handling
Airborne dust emissions generated during the handling, transport and mixing of dry materials will
be reduced by modernizing the existing facilities and implementation of a 'clean plant program'.
6.2.1.1.6 Waste Material Storage
Details of provisions to reduce wind borne dust emissions from open storage areas are provided
in Sections 6.2.1.3 and 6.2.2.3.
6.2.1.1.7 Planning Strategy for the Reduction of Air Emissions
A five year plan has been developed for the implementation of programmes to bring air
emissions into compliance with legislative requirements, and is shown in Figure 6.2.1. At the
end of this five year period, air emission levels will be at or below current limits set by the
Bulgarian Environmental Ministry and the draft BAT-EU recommendation of Osparcom.

During this period, a general trend of reducing air emission levels is expected. However, given
the extent of changes to be made to smelter operations and equipment, combined with an
increase in productions levels, air emissions are expected to fluctuate over short term periods.
The planning strategy foresees . urgent protective measures tto be taken within 6 months, the
major operational investments and controlled measures within the first 3 years. Further
optimalisation and management of the production and cleaning installations, may require an
additional two years period. The final objective will be to comply with both Bulgarian and EU-
BAT standards within 5 years from the signing of the agreement.

6.2.1.2 Waste Water
A two-step approach will be followed in order to remedy MDK's effluent water problems:
Step 1: Proposed Intermediate Solution and Remedies
Step 2: Future Design Improvements
Step 1: Proposed Intermediate Solution and Remedies of the Problems
Step 1. 1: Existing Neutralization Plant Design Improvements (to realise within 6 months)
The following improvements to the existing effluent treatment plant at MDK are proposed for
immediate implementation.  The proposed "interim  design' will eliminate most of the
environmental problems and provide an effluent treatment plant which is consistent with Current
Industry Practice elsewhere in the Western World and Latin America.
- Keep the existing infrastructure, including acidic effluent equalisation, lime making and
neutralisation system.
- Improve the design of the neutralisation unit by rebuilding liquid risers to enable better
contacting, improve aeration and mixing by using air spargers, provide lime feed to each of
the three neutralisation chambers and install 3-stage pH control, one in each of the three
chambers.
- The operating conditions (i.e. aeration rate, pH, lime concentration and quantity, reaction
time and arsenic speciation) should be optimised in a parallel bench-top study in order to
"fine-tune" operation of the plant, optimise use of chemicals and improve quality of treated
effluent.
Step 1.2: New Design and Additional Equipment for Immediate Implementation (to realise
between 9 and 12 months)
The following additional equipment will bring about major improvement in the performance of the
water treatment process and the quality of effluent produced by the wastewater treatment plant
(see Figures 4.2-2, 6.2-land 6.2-2):
A large circular clarifier/thickener to be installed immediately after the existing neutralization
plant. The clarifier will separate the arsenic-contaminated solids from the water as a
thickened slurry of approximately 15-20% solids concentration.

- A large recessed plate filter press with adequate capacity to dewater the sludge under high
pressure and obtain a filter cake containing approximately 60-70% w/w dry solids. The filter
cake will be removed and transported by trucks to the three existing lagoons nearby, to be
dug up, prepared and lined so that they can safely receive the hazardous arsenic-containing
filter cake. By using a high pressure on the order of 15 bar, the filter cake will have low
moisture and thus will be easier to handle and occupy minimum space.
- The filtrate from the filter press as well as the clarifier supernatant are combined and sent to
a polishing filter (i.e. dual media filter) to remove entrained suspended solids and minimize
the concentration of arsenic and other heavy metals in the effluent.
- Before discharge, the filtered effluent is pH adjusted by adding acid in a stirred tank, thus
reducing the pH to the range 7-8. The adjustment of pH of the filtered water will stop any
further precipitation of solids and result in an environmentally acceptable effluent.
This design for immediate implementation represents a well-recognized and accepted industry
practice for the treatment of acidic copper smelter effluents.
The above "immediate" improvements in the existing design can be implemented during a
period of approximately 9-12 months from privitatization of MDK. This period will be required to
prepare equipment specifications, purchase, install and start-up of the new equipment, as well
as preparation of the new, secure solid disposal lagoons.
Step 2: Future Design Improvements
The proposed "Intermediate Design" can be upgraded at a later date (i.e. within a period of three
years) in order to further improve the effluent treatment process and achieve even better
separation of arsenic and other heavy metals from the water. A multistage water treatment
system will be installed based on the Best Available Techniques.
The above water treatment system design will result in a dramatic improvement in the quality of
the effluent and will likely achieve an overall plant effluent arsenic concentration on the order of
0.1 mg/L (annual average) or 0.5 mg/L (daily average), compared to an average range of 1-5
mg/L which is achievable by using the simple neutralization, filtration, dewatering and pH
adjustment scheme which is proposed for immediate implementation. Clearly, the Future
Solution" will require considerable additional investment in equipment and a significant increase
in the plant operating costs.
Process Improvements
One of the key contributors to the large quantity of solids generated by the neutralization plant is
the relatively high concentration of the 'weak acid" generated as a result of gas cleaning and
cooling. The problem of metal dust removal is complicated by the high sulphur trioxide
PR90579 Rev 0. 9510                                                   PRMOT, R&v. 3. 9610
P Ujn.y,,n%9079.doclDo,rU0579 dOC



GIPL                                                      Union Miniere - EIA of MDK Smelter
HATCH                                                                        Page 127
concentration in the gas from the flash furnace. This adds to the acid value in the"weak acid"
waste and to calcium sulphate production as by-product of acid neutralization. Therefore:
Operation of the flash furnace must therefore be optimized to reduce S03 production.
Futhermore, the feasibility of recycling some of the weak acid streams will be considered
(e.g. from the wet ESP's).
The feasibility of reusing the water used for regenerating the Ion Exchange resins for the
purpose of gas cleaning and cooling should also be examined.
Finally, it is worthwhile also to consider using the effluent from the proposed effluent
treatment plant for cooling of the slag since this application requires a relatively poor quality
water.
Surface Water Run-off and Rainwater
In general, the quality of surface run-off from the MDK plant is not poor. The pH in the Sanar
Dere, Kiselo Dere, Zlatitsa and Pirdopska rivers are in the range 6.5-9 and the TSS is in the
range 10-80 mg/L, with the rivers having less than 50 mg/L as TSS. The concentration of heavy
metals based on the recent audit conducted on-site were quite low, except for copper ranging
between 0.2 and 2 mg/L. Current analyses of zinc (Zn) show a concentration in the range 0.02 -
0.5 mg total Zn/L, with the lower range reflecting Zlatishka, Pirdopska and Topolnitza rivers.
The lower values of zinc are below the aquatic toxicity levels for most organisms.
From examination of the limited analytical data available todate, it is difficult to conclude whether
or not the run-off surface water will need treatment. It is expected that the quality of surface
water will improve significantly after implementing measures to clean the MDK site and to
improve the process and control various emissions and dust. The concentration of metals such
as copper, zinc, arsenic, etc. will likely decrease significantly. At that point, it will be possible to
determine whether or not surface water catchment and treatmnent  will be necessary.
Consideration should be given to water conservation, reduction of surface run-off, recycling,
reuse, etc.

6.2.1.3 Solid Waste Disposal
6.2.1.3.1 General
The process of disposal of solid wastes will be developed in three stages:
Step 1.     Immediate Remediation that will be required to ensure public safety and allow
continued operation.
Step 2.      Intermediate disposal plan to reduce the existing pollution in the shortest time.
This will allow time to design the future solution and finalize acquisition of land.
Step 3.     Future disposal plan that will meet the Bulgarian and International criteria. Future
disposal facilities have to become available about 3 years after Union Miniere
becomes investor in the plant.
The three steps of the disposal plans for gypsum arsenite solids, fayalite tailings and re-usable
plant residues are illustrated on Drawings 90579-02, 90579-003 and 90579-004. The concepts
and conceptual desiyns are discussed in the next sections.

6.2.1.3.2 Solid waste from acid effluent treatment plant.
Step 1: Immediate action to create an intermediate disposal area (to realise within 6 months)
The solid wastes will be safely disposed in plastic lined trenches located in the small lagoons
area.
The existing slime pond represents a critical environmental risk, requiring urgent measures for
the following reasons:
It is practically full with a large volume of slimes containing arsenic and heavy metals. Due to
lack of any additional capacity, a major rainfall could cause an overflow or weakening and
breaking the dam, releasing arsenic slimes.
- The perimeter dam of the lagoon may not be stable. Analyses of the dam based on
recognized international standards, shows the minimum factor of safety of the dam being
below accepted criteria. A collapse of the dam would be catastrophic by releasing arsenic
and other contaminants into the Topolnitsa Reservoir.
- Because of the unavailability of an altemative discard area, the lagoon nonetheless
continues to be filled with additional contaminated slime.
It is of utmost urgency to fortify the dam immediately and develop an altemative area for
disposal of the calcium arsenite slime. UM/Hatch have develop a plan that can be executed
very rapidly if early approval is obtained. This plan consists of the following:
1. Increase the stability of the dam  immediately.  This will be done by fortressing the
downstream slope with additional earth material. This additional fill has been designed so
that it agrees with the final reclaim slope that will be produced when the slime is rehabilitated
into park land. The dam re-inforcement is illustrated on Drawing 90579-203.
The second part of the stabilization will involve placing a clay zone on the upstream side of
the dam, to depth of about 3 m, to stop the leakage of water through the dam. Continued
leakage will weaken the dam.
2. Develop an altemative plan to dispose of the calcium-arsenite. The proposed plan will
consist of modifying the existing neutralization plant to produce a solid filter cake residue and
the solid calcium-arsenite will be encapsulated within a plastic liner to form a secure disposal
site. The changes will involve the following:
Modifying the existing neutralization plant by:
adding a clarifier to settle out the neutralized material into a sludge;
PR90579 Rev 0. 95fa8                                                  PR DOT. Rev 3. 96105
uton905?9ndoc9or90s579 doe



ZXL                                                  Union Miniere - EIA of MOK Smelter
HATCH                                                                        Page 130
- adding high pressure filter presses that will compress the sludge into a solid filter cake
that can be handled as earth material, and
- adding multi-media filter to remove fine suspended solids from the clarifier and filter
press effluents.
The existing small lagoons will be rehabilitated and upgraded to store safely the solid
residue This will be done by completing the excavation of all residues within the lagoons,
deepening the lagoons by one metre to increase their capacity, installing a groundwater
drainage system and than finally lining the lagoon with a heavy duty polyethylene (plastic)
liner. The solid residue will be placed from end, covered continually with a soil layer and a
plastic liner. Finally, the liner will be covered with a minimum of 1 m fill. This will
encapsulate the calcium-arsenite.
It is necessary to immediately order the equipment for the neutralization plant and the lagoon
liners and start preparing the lagoons for the solid residue. Utmost urgency will be required in
order to complete the modification so that the disposal of the slime into the slime pond can be
stopped as soon as possible.
The immediate plan for the residue will produce storage for only about two years. A new
intermediate and long-term disposal area will have to be developed.
The environmental hazard of the slime pond, Blue Lagoon, has been known for some time, and
various solutions to solve the problem have been considered. All the solutions involve mixing
the calcium-arsenite slime with fayalite tailings and pumping this slurry to an off-site tailings
pond such as the existing pond at Chelopetch mine, into underground mine openings at
Chelopetch or constructing a new pond at Bakadjik.
UM/Hatch do not accept these solutions for several reasons as follows:
- diluting the arsenic with fayalite is not acceptable by intemational standards because it does
not solve the problem of arsenic, but spreads it,
- transporting the arsenic/fayalite creates another risk of pipe leakage that will result in spills in
agricultural land and there will be continuous work along the pipeline route because the
great abrasiveness of the fayalite tailings will require pipe repairs and frequent replacement,
- capital cost and operating costs for the pipeline and pumping will be high,
finally, UM/Hatch disagree in developing a disposal site on virgin land surrounded by
agricultural land when a better altemative is available, nor does UM/Hatch agree in placing
the mixture within an underground mined-out area.  The reason being that normally
groundwater will seep through the filled-in mine openings which will contaminate the
groundwater and most ministries of mines do not want to contaminate future ore resolves.
UM/Hatch propose to continue the disposal process design developed for the immediate
solution. It is proposed to continue producing solid filter cake residue and encapsulating in

plastic lined trenches. These new trenches would be located just south of the plant and east of
the slime lagoon in an area which has been largely used for disposal of solid construction
materials. The intermediate and future residue disposal would be done in lined trenches that will
be constructed as needed and continually covered.
The trenches will be perpendicular to an existing road. To minimize any impact on the public
using this road, it is proposed to provide a buffer between the residue disposal area and the
road and to construct on the buffer, i.e. between the road and disposal area, a landscape
longitudinal mound that will prevent direct view of the residue disposal area.
The plan for future solid residue disposal is shown on Drawing 90579-03.
In the future (step 3) the new water treatment plant will produce two streams of solid waste,
namely; gypsum and calcium ferric arsenate. Both of these wastes will be compressed into
solid residue cakes that will be safely disposed in similar but separate lined trenches. The
trenches will be located south of the plant and east of the existing slime lagoon.
The trenches will be progressively excavated as needed, the sections that are filled, will be
continuously covered and reclaimed. Only small sections of one set of trenches will be exposed
at any time.
6.2.1.3.3 Fayalite Tailings
Step 1: Immediate Remediation: After settling and dewatering in a pond, the newly produced
tailings will be excavated and transported to the Elatzite tailings area for disposal. As an
immediate action, the transportatioh of the tailings to Elatzite, will be performed by means of
covered trucks.
A temporally settling pont, will be created and used until the final tailing disposal site (see step 2)
will be ready (within 5 years).
Special provisions will be made for the design of the intermediate settling area to prevent wind
erosion. Some of the measures that will be taken are:
-  providing sufficient freeboard between the maximum level of tailings and the crest of the
perimeter dams,
-  discharge the tailings in the active pond along a central pipe to maximize the saturation of
the tailings and maintaining water cover over the lower two thirds of the tailings ( i.e. keep a
minimum of two thirds of the tailings submerged), and
- provide water spray over the upper one third exposed tailings.
PR9OS79 Re.. 0. 950                                                     PR.OT. Rev. 3. 965
p:Aun)nmwn90579%oca='90579.doc

HATCH                                                                         Page 132
Step 2: Intermediate Disposal: In the future a new designed tailing disposal facility complying to
the requirements of the new EU landfill directive, will be constructed.
The operation of two small ponds to sediment out the tailings and trucking the tailings to the
Elatzite tailings pond was only a temporary and interim solution. It is proposed to develop a
better solution for the long term condition. The disposal of the tailings has been studied for
many years but up to the present time, no solution has been accepted by the Ministry of
Environment. Some of the solutions that have been considered are:
- pumping the tailings to either Chelopetch mine tailings pond or place these tailings into
underground mined out areas,
- designing a new tailings pond in Bakadjik area, and
- selling the fayalite tailings to the cement industry.
Step 3: Future Disposal Plan: UM/Hatch have considered these options and found them
undesirable when compared to their preferred option for the following reasons:
Chelopetch tailings pond or underground mine.
1) Pumping of fayalite tailings over long distances will involve considerable maintenance of the
pipeline and be costly to operate. Frequent maintenance of the pipeline and the potential for
spills will not be looked at favourably by the local population.
2) The disposal of tailings into another mine with a different owner is not a secure option.
3) Most mine operators have found pumping of tailings into mine openings costly and
inefficient. This method is most often used when it is necessary to reinforce the mined out
areas for additional ore recovery. In this case the tailings are mixed with cement to produce
a rigid roof support.
New tailings pond at Bakadjik.
1) UM/Hatch disagree in developing a disposal site on virgin land surrounded by agricultural
land when a better alternative is available.
2) The proposed Bakadjik site will be costly to develop because it will require high dams. It is
questionable if low cost soil will be available for the construction of the dams. If not, more
costly rock will have to be used.
3) The Bakadjik site will require several kilometre long pipelines that will have to cross
agricultural land. The pipeline will require continuous maintenance and therefore an access
road which will impact on the agricultural land it will cross.
Future Fayalite Tailings Pond adjacent to MDK Plant
UM/Hatch prefer to develop a future tailings pond immediately adjacent to the MDK plant. The
proposed area is bounded on two sides by the existing slag dump and the large filled fayalite
tailings pond. The advantages of this area are:

1 ) It is hidden from Pirdop citizens by the existing high slag dump,
2) It is bounded on two sides by existing plant deposits, namely: slag and fayalite tailings. This
area is not used by the public for through access.
3) Large quantities of soil will be required to rehabilitate the slime pond, slag dump, existing
large tailings pond, and fill many depressions at the plant that were produced by the
excavation of contaminated soils. The resulting depression will be a start of the tailings pond.
4) Because of the great depth of soil overburden at this location, plenty of soil will be needed to
construct a perimeter dam on the east side. The construction of dams on the south and
west side will require only small amount of fill because of the existence of the walls from the
slag dump and tailings pond.
5) The final closure, rehabilitation of this pond, will merge into the rehabilitated slag dump and
tailings pond to form one mound.
At this time, UM/Hatch have developed an intermediate solution for handling the fayalite tailings.
Upon completion of the design of this intermediate solution, UM will re-evaluate the solutions
presented by others and consider new solutions.
6.2.1.3.4 Residue Storage Disposal Area
The existing disposal area for various plan residues that are re-used in the smelter are stored
within a concrete walled compound in the western section of the plant. The compound floor
consist of asphalt paving over natural compacted soil. One drill hole within the compound, and
drilling outside along the perimeter, show soil and some water contamination.
It is planned to construct a new floor within the compound with a surface water collection system
to prevent future soil contamination. This work will be step 2 project.
The construction will involve:
- removal of the asphalt and excavation of about 1 m of the contaminated soil,
covering the soil with a heavy plastic liner,
- constructing a concrete floor in the compound, and
providing a surface drainage system so that all precipitation water can be collected in a
central location and removed for treatment and a water treatment plant.

The following schedule shown in Figure 6.2.1-1 indicates the timing for implementing the various
stages for improving the handling of solid wastes at MDK.

6.2.2 Remediation of Contaminated Areas and Disposal Sites
6.2.2. 1 General
MDK plant has four major contaminated areas/disposal sites that have to be remediated,
namely:
- contamination within the plant area
- slime pond
- fayalite tailings pond, and
- slag dump

These areas/disposal areas have to be remedied to prevent the contamination spreading to the
surrounding environment and prevent an environmental disaster in case of the failure of the
slime pond. A summary of the concern and the proposed remediation measures are discussed
in the next sections.
6.2.2.2 Plant Area Contamination
The plant is contaminated by the presence of hazardous solid waste piles, operation of various
facilities, such as, electrolysis plant, acid plant, fuel area and abandoned smelter production
buildings in various state of collapse. The major contaminants are heavy metals, such as As,
Cu, Pb, Cd, and Sb and hydrocarbons.
Materials and soils contaminated materials with these metals are sources for surface and
groundwater contamination which may migrate offsite. Therefore the sources have to excavated
and removed into a secure landfill.
The degree of contamination of the various areas and the contaminants were presented in
Section 4.6. Details of the environmental assessment of the plant is presented in detail in
Technical Support Document 3, Solid Wastes and Ground Conditions.
Estimated quantities of major contaminated and scrap steel at the plant site are given in Table
6.2.2-1. This table indicates that there are 565 tons of contaminated solid waste and 66,000 tons
of scrap steel.

The contaminated solid waste will be encapsulated in the slime pond and the scrap steel will be
clean, cut into suitable sizes and sold to the steel industry. The concept of waste encapsulation
is discussed in the next section.
Groundwater contamination was observed to be widespread in the Southwest corner of the site
near the abandoned smelter area, electrolysis workshop, small lagoons and hydrochloric acid
storage tanks. While the level of groundwater impact on the site is high, the mass transport of
contaminants off-site is relatively small.
However, considering the high groundwater contamination at the plant site, further studies of the
contamination flow and potential effect on Topotnitsa River will have to be evaluated in greater
detail.
6.2.2.3 Slime Pond
The environmental hazard of the slime pond, Blue Lagoon, has been know for some time, and
various solutions have been forwarded.  Three previously proposed solutions known to
UM/Hatch are:
- mixing the slimes with the tailings and pumping this slurry to an offsite tailings pond,
mixing the slimes with local clay soil and compacting this mixture into large mound and
,processing of the slimes through a gold extraction process and storing the resulting slimes in
a more secure containment.
UM and Hatch do not consider any of these as optimum solutions and is proposing an altemate
solution that is considered to be more environmentally acceptable. The relative advantages and
disadvantages of the alternative proposals are discussed in more detail below.
1) Pumping Slime/Tailings Mixture to an Off-Site Tailings Pond
These solutions involve mixing the calcium-arsenite slime with fayalite tailings and pumping
this slurry to an off-site tailings pond such as the existing pond at Chelopetch mine, into
underground mine openings at Chelopetch or construct a new pond at Bakadjk.UM/Hatch do
not accept this solutions for several reasons:
diluting the arsenic with fayalite is not acceptable by international standards because it does
not solve the problem of arsenic, but spreads it,
- transporting the arsenic/fayaiite creates another risk of pipe leakage that will result in spills in
agricultural land and there will be continuous work along the pipeline route because the
great abrasiveness of the fayalite tailings will require pipe repairs and frequent replacement,
- capital cost and operating costs for the pipeline and pumping will be high,
PR90579 Rev. 0. 95106                                                 PR. DOT. Rev 3. 96/05
p Inonm.%90579%o*Wx579 coc



Union Miniere - EIA of MDI< Smelter
HATCH                                                                          Page 137
>  finally, UM/Hatch disagree with the proposal to develop a disposal site on virgin land
surrounded by agricultural land when a better alternative is available, nor does UM/Hatch
agree in placing the mixture within underground mined-out area. The reason being that
normally groundwater will seep through the filled-in mine openings which will contaminate
the groundwater and most ministries of mines do not want to contaminate future ore
resolves.
2) Mixing slimes with clay soil and storing in a mound.
This solution is not acceptable because of the difficulty in construction, use of large volumes of
good clay soil and producing a much larger volume of contaminated material, albeit in a diluted
form. In brief:
-  mixing of slimes with clay soil will require large volumes of clay soil to soak up the large
volumes of water in the slimes,
- mixing will require a large outside surface area that will require a rainwater collection and
treatment system for the rainy periods and that will produce wind erosion during dry weather,
- mixing of the large volume of clay required for the mixing, the end result will be a much
larger volume of wastes, albeit less contaminated,
- the mound will require a cover to prevent rainwater infiltration and contamination of the
groundwater in the long-term.
3) Gold Extraction
It is proposed to dredge out the slimes from the pond and process the slimes through a cyanide
process to extract gold and other precious metals that are contained in the slimes.  It is
suggested that the income from the recovery of gold and precious metals will pay for the safe
disposal of the remaining arsenic slimes. This solution is not acceptable to the Union Miniere
team for the following reasons:
-  the cyanide gold extraction process will add another pollutant to the slimes,
-  it is believed that the plant to extract the gold and precious metals is too costly for the
relatively small volume of slimes and the estimated gold content to make any appreciable
profit, if any, to provide money for disposal of the slimes,
-  the cyanide gold processing of gypsum slimes is not well proven technology,
-  the cyanide plant operation will produce an increased risk of contaminating the outside area,
and finally,
-  a separate slime storage area will have to be developed.
4) UM/Hatch Preferred Solution - Encapsulation of the Slime Pond
Union Miniere propose to leave the slime in place, compress it into a near solid state and cover
the slime and other covering material with a double liner to prevent future rain water infiltration.
The resulting mound would be covered with clean earth and vegetated with grass, bushes and
trees to produce a park.
PR90579 R*v. 0. 9508                                                    PRDOOT. Rev. 3. 96bV
p \un.mn%90579docsWPvr9O579 coc



z~i~7K                                                   Union Miniere - EIA of MDK Smelter
HATCH                                                                        Page 138
The solution would consist of the following components:
- Termination of Slime Disposal in the Blue Lagoon. In a very short time after Union Miniere
becomes a full investor in the MDK Plant, it will improve the acid plant effluent treatment
plant and incorporate slime filter presses that will produce small volumes of solid"slimes" to
be disposed in lined secured storage containments.
- Perimeter Dam Reinforcement.  The perimeter will be reinforced as soon as possible to
provide additional comfort in regard to the stability of the dam for the period until the
encapsulation of the Blue Lagoon is completed. The proposed reinforcement will form the
lower portion of the final vegetated mound.
- In-Place Compression of Slimes. The slimes in the Blue lagoon will be compressed in-place
into a solid mass that will have about half of the existing volume and thickness. This will be
executed with proven technologies that are used to build roads over soft soils and
consolidate very soft slimes and clays. To consolidate the slime, a heavy loading will have
to be applied to the surface of the slime by placing earth fill. To minimize the need to bring
new material to the plant, it is proposed to use some of the contaminated materials from the
MDK plant site as the loading material.
Encapsulating the Compressed Slimes and Contaminated Plant Fill Material. It is proposed
to shape the fill over the Blue Lagoon to form a natural looking mound that can be developed
into a park setting. The mound will be covered with double protection consisting of a plastic
liner and a 'dry cover" to stop any rain infiltration into the contaminated materials and slimes.
The "dry cover" will have a thick soil cover that will be vegetated with grass , bushes and
trees.
- Perimeter underground drainage system will designed and constructed to prevent
contamination of the groundwater during construction and prevent future regional
groundwater being contaminated by the slimes.
The design for this solution is provided in Technical Support Document No 4, titled Slime
Lagoon. The concept of slime consolidation and encapsulation is illustrated on Drawing 90579-
206.
6.2.2.4 Fayalite Tailings Pond
The existing fayalite tailings pond has reached its capacity. It has been partially covered with
soil but the northern portion is still being used for alternatively settling out of tailings siurry. This
northem area is required for settling out the tailings because only one small pond is available for
this purpose. When this pond is being used, the northern portion of the large pond has to be
used as the alternative settling pond.
PR9OS79 Rev. 0. 95/08                                                 PR.OOT. Rev. 3. 96A)S
p Wnonmnk9O579kdocssO%73 coc



Z~L - lUnion Miniere - EIA of MDK Smelter
HATCH                                                                       Page 139
UM is proposing to construct a second small settling pond just west of the existing small pond so
that the large tailings pond can be closed and remediated. This will be readily accomplished
since the fayalite tailings are inert. Groundwater sampling at the toe of the tailings pond did not
show any groundwater contamination.
The closure or remediation of the tailings pond will involve some regrading of the slope to make
the southern slope shallower for vegetation and aesthetic purpose, regrade the surface to
control the surface water and vegetate the new regraded mound.
The design of the small tailings pond, regrading of the large tailings pond and the conceptual
design of a proposed future tailings pond are given Technical Support Document No 5, titled
Fayalite Tailings Disposal.
6.2.2.5 Slag Dump
The slag dump contains some 7 million tonnes of slag and covers an area of about 11 ha. The
slag dump surface is irregular, black in colour and presents an unsightly feature above the town
of Pirdop.  Aside of the aesthetic features, it is not environmentally friendly because of
production of dust during high wind and the high content of copper. The copper is released
gradually and produces unacceptable seepage water. However, the release is not of major
proportion.
It is proposed to regrade the dump to produce gradual slopes to provide a stable slope to
support vegetation. The slag dump will be covered with a soil layer representing a 'dry cover'.
The 'dry cover' design is used for mine waste rock that generate acid because of high sulphur
content. The 'dry cover' design consist of two layers of clayey soil, similar to that which is found
upslope of the slag dump. The bottom layer is about 0.6 m thick and is compacted. The upper
layer is placed loosely.
The concept is that the lower layer forms a low permeability zone that keeps the water in the
upper layer. The upper layer acts as a water storage zone in which rainfall water is stored to
evaporate at a later time. This combination reduces the infiltration of water greatly and keeps
the oxygen out of the mine rock, or in this case, slag. The great reduction of water infiltration
into the mine rock or slag, reduces seepage contamination by the reduction of water and the
absence of oxygen.
6.3 Recommendations for Improvement of Environmental Management
6.3.1 Structure
The structure of environmental management at MDK is discussed in Section 3. Although there
is a system in place for monitoring air emissions and waste water discharges it is not clear how
the information is distributed to the departments responsible.  For example, although the
environmental manager is responsible for monitoring environmental emissions, liaising with the
PR9OS79 Rev 0. O95t                                                  PR. DOT. R- 3. 9SMS
p%unmonnn90579Wd O 579.doc



jGIs.                                                    Union Miniere - EIA of MDK Smelter
HATCH                                                                        Page 140
regulatory authorities and ensuring that the plant is in compliance with the regulations, the
responsibility for control of emissions should be at Plant or Department Manager level. For this
to be effective a formal system should be established for reporting violations or non -compliance
to the Departmental Managers.
6.3.2 Management Control Techniques
Management control techniques are an important factor in minimising fugitive emissions.
Specific techniques under this category include:
plant housekeeping
training and contingency planning
inspection schedules and maintenance
- minimisation of waste
6.3.2. 1 Housekeeping
From visual observations made during the assessment there is no attention to housekeeping on
the plant; dust and waste materials are accumulating inside and outside the plant areas.
Dust that accumulates in yards or buildings can be a significant source of fugitive emissions.
The risk can be reduced by paving surface areas. Outside paved areas should be cleaned
using a mobile vauum cleaner; indoor areas can be cleaned using smaller equipment.
Equipment for cleaning spills or leaks should be readily available.
Housekeeping should be an integral part of the daily operating routine and systems should be
introduced to monitor its effectiveness. Supervisors and operators must be aware of their
individual responsibility towards protecting the environment.
6.3.2.2 Training and Contingency Planning
The training of operators and supervisors should emphasise the potential environmental
problems associated with a particular operation. Written procedures should be available with
specific action to be'taken in the event of process malfunction or equipment failure which could
result in in release of pollutants to the environment.
Records of employee training in this respect should be maintained and refresher training carried
out at regular intervals.
6.3.2.3 Inspection schedules and maintenance
Pollution abatement equipment should incorporate the necessary control' and alarm
instrumentation to warn of breakdown or excursion outside preset limits.
PR90579 Rev 0. 95108                                                  PR DOT. Rev. 3. 96105
D unanm,\90579XdoC579 doc



ZGIQPJ\                                                  Union Miniere - EIA of MDK Smelter
HATCH                                                                       Page 141
In addition, procedures should be employed to ensure that routine manual or visual checks are
also carried out to guarantee correct operation or condition of abatement equipment. These
checks should be recorded. These inspections should include for example:
- the condition of flues and ducts for accumulation of dust
- the condition of seals, hatches and dampers for signs of fume leakage
- the operation of alarm systems
Preventative maintenance should be practiced to replace or recondition critical equipment
prior to its anticipated failure date.
6.3.2.4 Waste Minimisation
A thorough audit of individual processes should be carried out to determine the potential for
recycle of water, solid waste and packaging. Dry or dusty materials should be handled in closed
containers.
6.4 Measures for Monitoring Emissions
6.4. 1 Air
6.4.1.1 Emissions
Point sources of proces emissions where monitoring is required to record the levels of
substances requiring control are:
- the combined gas stream to the 325 metre stack for: dust and gaseous substances, sulphur
dioxide, nitrogen oxides and carbon monoxide. The dust will be analysed for representative
metals.
the combined gas stream from the sulphuric acid plants prior to the 120 metre stack for:
sulphur dioxide and sulphur trioxide
Air measurements will be best carried out to conform to techniques approved by the
Environmental Agencies.
6.4.1.2 Ambient Air Quality
MDK already monitors the concentration of sulphur dioxide in the atmosphere at two locations;
one in Pirdop and one in Zlatitsa. Additional instruments have been purchased so that
monitoring can be carried out at additional locations in these towns.
A programme for routine monitoring of dust in these locations and at various selected positions
at the plant perimeter will be implemented. Standard techniques for this include:
- deposit guages for meauring the quantity of deposited dust
- high volume air sampling to determine the concentration of fine particle and respirable dust
(<10 microns) in the atmosphere.

6.4.2 Water
6.4.2.1 Effluent waters
After installing the definitive waste water cleaning plant, a continuous measurement system of
water sampling, based on flow rate measurements, will be installed.
Measurements of all relevant and important parameters will be conducted, in accordance to
Bulgarian and international requirements.
6. 4.2 Receiving surface waters
Frequent controls of upstream and downstream water samples will be taken from the
conjunction of the Pirdopska into the Topolnitsa river, to assess and evaluate the impact of the
MDK plant on the receiving water quality.

7. CONCLUSIONS
In general the Environmental Impact Assessment carried out by Hatch Union/Miniere confirms
the findings of previous impact assessments and studies.  The significant issues are
summarised in the following sections. The proposed plans for remediation of the most serious
contamination and for bringing the plant into compliance with Bulgarian and European
evironmental legislation are also summarised.
7.1 Environmental Impact
7.1.1 Air
- sulphur dioxide emissions from the acid plant stack are in excess of the mandatory limits due
to the relatively low conversion efficiency of the single contact acid plant.
- emissions of dust and sulphur dioxide from the 325 metre stack exceed the environmental
standards. However considering the height of the release point these are not considered to
have a significant impact on local air quality.
-  low level fugitive emissions from the furnace operations and dust blown from dry storage
areas have a significant impact on the ambient air quality both inside and outside the plant
perimeter.
7.1.2 Water
- the treated acidic effluent which is discharged to the river Pirdopska exceeds the Category 3
receiving water standards for several parameters
the drainage water from the operating facility which is discharged to the Sanar Dere channel
often exceeds the crieria for Category 3 receiving water
- run-off water from the old part of the site plus water from the slag treatment process which
are discharged into the Kiselo Dere channel exceed the crteria for Category 2 receiving
water
7.1.3 Solid Waste
The major concems with respect to solid waste storage and disposal are:
- the receptacle for the waste water treatment sludge is almost full and there are concems
about its stability
- the fayalite tailings pond which is now full is a source of windblown dust emissions
- the presence on site of a variety of waste materials, contaminated disused equipment and
derelict structures and buildings is a source of ground and groundwater contamination
7.2 Remediation
A five year plan has been developed for bringing the plant into compliance with regulations and
for remediation of existing waste storage areas and contaminated parts of the site
A contributing factor to the exceedance of limits for air emissions is the current operating
regime of the smelter. A priority for UM would be to operate the smelter with a consistent
throughput of higher grade copner concentrate so that it is runnino at a level close to its
PR90579 Rev. 0. 95=W                                                  PRDOT. R*v. 3.96105
D \tnXn90579womx90579 doc



1~7L                                                     Union Miniere - EIA of MDK Smelter
HATCH                                                                        Page 144
design parameters. Optimisation of the smelter operation in this manner will improve the
efficiency of the gas handling and cleaning system. At the same time UM will study the
modification or replacement of gas cleaning equipment necessary to bring the plant into
compliance.
A two step approach will be adopted for improving the treated effluent quality. In the first
step for immediate implementation, the existing neutralisation plant will be modified to
improve the efficiency and additional equipment will be installed so that a solid filter cake
residue is produced instead of a slurry. The filter cake will be deposited safely in lined
trenches. In the second step the plant will be upgraded to a double stage treatment process
based on best available technique for treatment of effluent of this type. Furthermore the
optimisation of the smelter operation and gas cleaning is expected to reduce the volume of
acidic effluent requiring treatment.
-- Since the pond used for storing waste water treatment sludge is full and there are concems
about the stability of the walls, provision will be made to store this material in a suitably
designed lagoon. This will be linked with the improved effluent treatment process discussed
above.
>- the pond will be decommissioned and then encapsulated
> alil of the waste on site, including disused buildings will be deposited onto the slime pond
to aid the dewatering and compression process
the fayalite tailings pond will be encapsulated so that it ceases to be an environmental risk
as far as dust emissions are concerned. Similarly an old slag dump will also be covered to
prevent any leachate from contaminating the sub-surface
